Done — matched tiles now flash for about 0.6 seconds before being destroyed, and every cascade wave gets the same treatment instead of resolving invisibly in one frame.

What changed in `obj_GridManager.json` (the side file wins over the stale embedded copy in `project.json`, I verified the loader's merge replaces `events` wholesale):

- **Click handler** no longer destroys anything. After a valid swap it just finds the matched tiles, stores them in `self.marked`, and arms a 36-frame timer (`self.flash`, ~0.6 s at the project's 60 fps). A swap with no match still reverts instantly, as before. Clicks are ignored while a flash is in progress.
- **New `step` event** counts the timer down; when it expires it removes the marked tiles, collapses/refills the columns, credits the score, and re-scans — if the drop created a new match, that wave is marked and flashed too, so chain reactions play out visibly wave by wave.
- **Draw event** renders marked tiles with a triple white outline and blinks their fill to white every 6 frames, so it's obvious which three-in-a-row is about to go. Score is only added at actual destruction time, so the score readout stays in sync with what the player sees.
- The match-scan logic was factored into a `find_matches` helper defined in the create event (it persists on the instance via `execute_code`'s locals handling, which I confirmed against the runtime).

I verified it headlessly with a 21-check harness driving the real `ActionExecutor` (rigged grids, real click/step/draw events): marking without destruction, input lockout during the flash, blink on/off frames, mid-flash grid immutability, clear + column collapse on expiry, cascade re-arming, instant revert on failed swaps, and the win path — 21/21 passed. Run the game from the IDE and the flash should be visible on your next match.

## [2026-07-03] User

> when the tiles cascade down after a group of 3 or more is destroyed, make the tiles slide down visibly

### Claude

Now I'll add a visible falling phase: when the flash ends, instead of teleporting tiles to their new cells, the grid is set to its final layout but each moved tile carries a pixel offset that shrinks every frame, so tiles slide down (new tiles drop in from above the board, clipped at the top edge). Input stays locked until the slide finishes, and cascade re-scan happens only after landing.

Now the draw event — apply each tile's fall offset and clip at the board's top edge:

Now I'll rewrite the harness to cover the fall phase (offsets, input lockout, draw clipping, landing, cascade re-scan after landing):

Done — after the match flash, tiles now visibly slide down into place instead of snapping. All 21 checks in the headless harness (driving the real `ActionExecutor`) pass.

How it works, continuing the state machine from the flash change:

- **When the flash expires**, the step event computes the final grid layout as before, but every tile that moved gets an entry in a new `self.falling` dict — its pixel distance above its resting cell. Surviving tiles that drop N rows start N×96 px high; brand-new tiles start stacked above the board (one full board-gap offset), so they pour in from the top edge.
- **Each frame**, the step event shrinks every offset by `self.fall_speed` (12 px/frame at 60 fps, so one row takes ~0.13 s and a full-column refill lands in ~0.4 s). The cascade re-scan now runs only when everything has landed — if the drop formed a new match, that wave flashes and then slides, so chains play out flash → slide → flash → slide.
- **The draw event** renders each tile at its offset position, hiding tiles still fully above the board and clipping partially-emerged ones at the top edge so they appear to slide out from under the header rather than popping in over the score text.
- **Clicks are ignored** while tiles are in motion (same lockout as during the flash), and a failed swap still reverts instantly with no fall. Score and the win check still happen at destruction time, unchanged.

The harness verified the exact offset math (288 px for the three refill tiles in the rigged column, 96 px for tiles above a mid-column hole, nothing below or beside it moves), the 24-step landing time, per-frame shrinkage, top-edge clipping in the draw queue, input lockout, and cascade re-arming at landing. Run the game and you should see the tiles flash, then rain down.

## The Android answer first, since that was the open question

**The game cannot ship on Android as it is written — and it would fail silently.** The APK pipeline itself is fine (`export/android/android_exporter.py` wraps the Kivy export and drives buildozer, via WSL on Windows), but I traced the generated Kivy runtime and it lacks the three things this game is built on:

- **`draw` events are never called.** The exported scene loop has a "DRAW EVENTS" comment where the call should be, `self._draw_queue` is never created, and nothing renders it. The exported `GameObject` renders sprites only — and our controller has no sprite, so the board would be a blank screen.
- **Mouse events are silently dropped.** The exporter's event map has no `mouse_*` entries (unmapped events are skipped), `self.mouse_x`/`mouse_y` are never populated, and on Android touch only reaches the virtual D-pad — never game instances. Taps would do nothing.
- **`execute_code` semantics are thinner** than the IDE's (no locals copy-back, no `game`/`keyboard` bindings). Our code survives this one, but it's part of the same parity gap.

Nothing in CI would catch this: the Kivy tests are codegen-string tests and the Android test mocks the build. I wrote all of this up as a new **"Kivy/Android export" entry in [TODO.md](TODO.md)** with the exact fix list (draw-queue renderer on the Kivy base object, call `on_draw` per frame, map `mouse_left_press` to touch, populate mouse coords). Until that lands, the honest position — now documented for users — is: Android export supports sprite+action games like the mazes; this game is desktop-only. The planned sprite-based **match3_2** is the natural path to a mobile-ready version.

## The game now ships as a bundled sample

- **[samples/match3_1/](samples/match3_1/)** — copied from your Documents project (minus the three stale AI "fix report" files), with the flash and slide animations included.
- **Complete documentation in [samples/match3_1/README.md](samples/match3_1/README.md)**: how to play, project structure, a full code walkthrough (state table, the idle → flash → fall → cascade state machine diagram, per-event behavior), tuning knobs, the match3_2/match3_3 roadmap, and the export-status section including the Android limitations. [CREDITS.txt](samples/match3_1/CREDITS.txt) dedicates the four tile sprites to CC0, matching the maze_1 convention.
- **Registered everywhere a sample needs to be**: the Welcome tab ("Match-3 — Level 1" in [welcome_tab.py](widgets/welcome_tab.py#L46)), the label-translation script (ran it — fr/de/es/it/ru/sl/uk added, "Три в ряд" for ru/uk, `.qm` recompiled), the [samples/README.md](samples/README.md) table (noted as the first natively-authored sample), and the headless smoke runner.

## Verification

The export genuinely couldn't work — and rather than just documenting that, I've now fixed the Kivy exporter itself. The exported match3_1 plays a full round (tap-select, swap, flash, slide, score) when driven headlessly through the generated code.

## What was missing and what I added to [kivy_exporter.py](export/Kivy/kivy_exporter.py)

- **Draw events never ran.** The exported scene loop's "step 8: DRAW EVENTS" was just a comment, and nothing created or rendered `self._draw_queue` — a sprite-less object like our grid controller rendered a blank screen. Now the base `GameObject` initializes the queue and has a `_render_draw_queue` that renders the IDE runtime's exact command schema (rectangle, circle, ellipse, line, text, scaled_text — colors as 0–255 tuples or `#RRGGBB`) into Kivy canvas instructions, converting from the game's y-down room coordinates to Kivy's y-up frame. The scene loop now calls `on_draw` and renders the queue every frame.
- **Mouse events were silently dropped.** The event map had no `mouse_*` entries and `self.mouse_x`/`mouse_y` never existed. Now `mouse_left_press`/`_button`/`_down` and `mouse_left_release` export to real methods, and the scene's touch handlers dispatch them with IDE-runtime semantics (every instance with the handler gets the event, no hit-test, `mouse_x`/`mouse_y` in room coordinates). The coordinate conversion inverts Android's fullscreen-scaling canvas transform (which Kivy does *not* apply to touches) and the desktop DPI window scale.
- **Bonus fix:** the Android virtual D-pad overlay is now generated only for games that actually have keyboard events (`NEEDS_DPAD`). Match-3 is touch-only, so it no longer gets an arrow pad covering the bottom-right of the board and eating taps there.

## How it's verified

- A harness exports `samples/match3_1` through the real `KivyExporter`, `py_compile`s every generated module, and checks all the new machinery is present (17/17).
- A second harness **runs the exported game** against stub kivy modules: builds the scene, renders a frame (39 draw-queue rectangles, y-flip verified to the pixel — tile (0,0) lands at Kivy y=568), then plays a real round via `scene.on_touch_down`: select → swap → 36-frame flash with input locked → score +30 → tiles slide 288 px in 24 frames → board re-renders (14/14).
- Both are condensed into a permanent regression file, [tests/test_kivy_draw_queue_mouse_export.py](tests/test_kivy_draw_queue_mouse_export.py) (9 tests), with careful `sys.modules` isolation so the kivy stubs can't leak into other tests.
- Full suite: **1466 passed, 0 failed**.

[TODO.md](TODO.md), the [sample README](samples/match3_1/README.md), and [samples/README.md](samples/README.md) now reflect the supported status; the still-open parity gaps that don't affect this game (`execute_code` locals copy-back, right/middle mouse, `sprite`/`lives` draw-queue types) stay tracked in TODO.md.

One thing to check on your side: **what did "does not seem to work" look like for you?** If you clicked **Export → Android Package (.apk)** and the *build itself* failed (rather than producing a broken game), that's the environment path — the APK build shells out to buildozer, which on Windows runs through WSL, so it needs WSL with buildozer installed. Note also the Export dialog's "Mobile (APK)" option only emits a Kivy/buildozer *project folder*, not an APK — only the Android-Package path actually compiles one. ## Why there was no .apk

I reproduced your exact experience by running the Android export end-to-end. The build ran fine for 40 minutes — WSL, buildozer, and the Android SDK/NDK on your machine are all set up correctly from your February attempt — and then **the exporter killed it with its own hardcoded 40-minute timeout** while python-for-android was still legitimately cross-compiling CPython. No `.apk`, misleading "try again, it'll be faster" message.

Worse, retrying could never help: the WSL bridge ran every build in a **fresh throwaway temp directory**, so each retry restarted the 40+ minute compile from zero and hit the same timeout. On this machine the export was mathematically unable to succeed.

## What I fixed in the exporter

- **Persistent per-project build directory** ([wsl_bridge.py](export/android/wsl_bridge.py)): builds now run in `~/.pygm/android_builds/<project>` inside WSL, keeping the python-for-android and gradle caches between runs. First build is slow once; rebuilds take minutes. The `game/` sources and `bin/` output are refreshed each run (clearing `bin/` also guarantees a stale APK from an old build can't be passed off as a new one).
- **Timeouts are now hang-failsafes, not build estimates** ([android_exporter.py](export/android/android_exporter.py)): 40 min → 3 h for WSL, 20 min → 2 h native, and the timeout message now says the truth — the cache is kept and a retry resumes.
- Regression coverage in [tests/test_android_wsl_persistent_build.py](tests/test_android_wsl_persistent_build.py) (6 tests). Full suite: **1471 passed, 0 failed**.

## Status right now

The real APK build for match3_1 is running again in the background with these fixes — currently compiling the Android toolchain recipes. It has to redo the compile once (the killed build's work was in a temp dir WSL already discarded — the very bug I fixed), so expect roughly 40–90 minutes. I'll report when it finishes, and the `.apk` will land in `C:\Users\gthul\Desktop\match3_android\`.

One clarification from your message: the Android option isn't a top-level menu item — it's **Build → Export Game...**, then the "Android Package (.apk)" radio button in the dialog (fifth in the list). It's present in your build of the IDE; on this machine it shows "✅ Available (via WSL)".

## Documentation: created

**[docs/ANDROID_EXPORT.md](docs/ANDROID_EXPORT.md)** — a complete classroom-oriented guide covering: where the option is (Build → Export Game… → Android Package), one-time setup per OS (with the exact `apt install` line for the Linux machines), realistic build times (first build 40–90 min, rebuilds a few minutes, and why), where every gigabyte lives (`~/.buildozer` ≈ 2 GB shared, `~/pygm_builds/<project>` ≈ 5–6 GB per project), installing the debug APK on tablets, and troubleshooting. The match3_1 sample README now links to it.

## Your question: can a Linux machine continue the APK work a week later, after logout/shutdown?

**Yes — as long as the student's home directory persists between sessions.** After today's fixes, all build state lives in the user's home (`~/.buildozer` for the SDK/NDK, `~/pygm_builds/<project>` for the compiled toolchain and gradle caches). Logging out or shutting down loses nothing; a week later the same student re-runs the export and:

- if the toolchain compile had finished, the rebuild takes **minutes**;
- if the build was interrupted mid-compile, python-for-android **resumes at the level of completed components** — finished work is not redone.

Three caveats that matter specifically for a school setup:

1. **This was impossible before today.** The exporter used to build in a throwaway temp directory with a 40-minute kill timer — every attempt restarted from zero and was killed mid-compile (that's exactly the "no .apk" failure you and I both hit). The persistence is the fix I landed this session, so classroom machines need this version of pygm.
2. **Ephemeral profiles break it.** If the school machines reset accounts on logout (guest sessions, disk-freeze software), every build is a first build. Persistent per-student accounts are needed; count ~8 GB of home-directory quota per student who builds an APK.
3. **Caches can't be seeded across users.** The build workspace embeds absolute `/home/<user>/…` paths, so copying a pre-built cache from a teacher account to student accounts breaks packaging (I verified this the hard way — moving the cache mid-session broke a path in `dist_info.json`, which I had to patch). Each account builds its own; only the first session is slow.

The build is not a background job — closing the IDE stops it — but stopping is always safe; the next export continues from the cache. The guide spells all of this out for whoever runs the classroom.

I'll confirm as soon as the running build delivers the `.apk` (expected within minutes — it's in the last phase). Suite state: 1472 passed, 0 failed with the new regression tests (persistent dot-free build dir for both WSL and native Linux, timeout failsafes).

## [2026-07-04] User

> Can the Linux IDE compile an executable for an iPhone?

### Claude

No. That's an Apple restriction, not a pygm one — and the IDE's iOS exporter reflects it honestly: [ios_exporter.py](export/ios/ios_exporter.py) hard-checks for macOS and refuses elsewhere ("iOS export requires macOS with Xcode installed"). In the Export dialog on a Linux machine, the iOS option shows "⚠️ Requires macOS with Xcode".

The reason it can't be worked around the way we did Android-on-Windows (via WSL): building an iPhone app requires Xcode's `xcodebuild`, the iOS SDK, and Apple's code-signing chain, all of which exist only on macOS and are licensed only for Apple hardware. There is no Linux equivalent of buildozer for iOS — kivy-ios (which our exporter drives) itself only runs on a Mac. And even a built app can't just be copied onto an iPhone like an APK: it must be signed against a provisioning profile tied to an Apple ID, and installed via Xcode or TestFlight.

For your classroom, realistic options in order of practicality:

1. **HTML5 export** — this is the answer for iPhones in a school setting. It builds fine from the Linux IDE and the game runs in Safari from a URL; no installation, no signing, no cables. Caveat: I haven't validated the HTML5 exporter against match3_1's draw-queue/mouse features the way I did the Kivy one — say the word and I'll do that verification pass.
2. **One Mac in the loop** — projects are portable folders, so students work on Linux and the final iOS build runs on a single Mac with Xcode. A free Apple ID can install onto a physically-connected iPhone, but the app expires after 7 days and must be re-signed; distributing to many students' phones properly needs a paid Apple Developer account (99 USD/year) and TestFlight. That's a lot of friction for a classroom.
3. **Android stays the easy native-mobile target** — the APK we just built installs on any Android tablet/phone with one tap and never expires.

If iPhones are common among your students, verifying the HTML5 export path is the highest-value next step — it's the only route that reaches every pocket regardless of OS.

---

## [2026-07-04] User

> At school the are Linux computer labs and Mac computer labs, would that be sufficient for all mobile platforms?

### Claude

Yes — hardware-wise that combination covers everything, with one platform-policy asterisk on the Apple side.

**Android — the Linux lab is fully sufficient.** Buildozer runs natively on Linux (no WSL layer needed like on this Windows box), and the persistent-cache fix I landed applies to the native path too: builds live in `~/pygm_builds/<project>`, survive logouts, and resume week to week. First build per student ~40–90 min, rebuilds in minutes, APKs install on any Android device with a tap. One honest caveat: I verified the full pipeline end-to-end via WSL here; the native-Linux branch is the same logic and is regression-tested, but it deserves one validation run on an actual school machine before you rely on it in a lesson.

**iOS — the Mac lab is sufficient for building, and mostly sufficient for the classroom.** The IDE's iOS exporter drives kivy-ios + `xcodebuild` and is designed to work with a **free Apple ID** signed into Xcode — no paid account needed to build and install onto a connected iPhone. The Mac lab needs Xcode installed (large download, ~15 GB — something for the lab admin to pre-install). The asterisks are Apple's rules, not ours:

- Apps signed with a free Apple ID **expire after 7 days** and must be rebuilt/reinstalled; max 3 such apps per device; the iPhone must be cable-connected to the Mac and "trusted".
- Each student needs an Apple ID usable in Xcode — check whether the school's managed Macs allow that.
- Distributing one game to many phones without cables (TestFlight) requires the paid Apple Developer Program (99 USD/year); accredited educational institutions can apply to Apple for a **fee waiver**, which might be worth pursuing.

Since projects are plain folders, the workflow across labs is simple: develop anywhere (Linux lab, or home), then carry the project to the Mac lab for the iOS build — Dropbox or a USB stick both work.

Two closing notes: first, the iOS exporter has had far less real-world exercise than the Android one — before planning a lesson around it, it's worth a validation session on one of the school Macs (I can prepare a checklist like the Android one in `docs/ANDROID_EXPORT.md`). Second, keep **HTML5 export** in your back pocket: it builds from either lab and reaches every student phone — Android or iPhone — through the browser with zero signing, zero cables, and zero expiry. For a mixed-device classroom, "native APK for Android + web build for iPhones" is often less friction than fighting Apple's 7-day resigning cycle.

**What the verification found.** It was broken in more ways than expected. The exported page's JavaScript engine had no `execute_code` support and no mouse events, so match3_1 exported to a dead page — and even **maze_1 couldn't move**: the two actions its player is built on (`test_alignment`, `start_moving_direction`) were silently dropped. Nobody had ever actually played an HTML5 export. The exporter also shipped stale game logic for folder projects, because it read object events from `project.json`'s embedded copies instead of the authoritative `objects/*.json` side files.

**What I built:**

- **Python in the browser.** When (and only when) a project contains `execute_code` actions, the exported page loads Pyodide (CPython compiled to WebAssembly) and runs the Python event code with the IDE runtime's exact semantics — persistent `self` attributes, locals copied back, `math`/`random`/`keyboard` available. Pure-action games like the mazes don't load it and stay fully offline.
- **Taps and clicks** dispatch as the left-mouse-press event with room-coordinate `mouse_x`/`mouse_y` — including `touchstart`, so it works on phones, which was the whole point for your iPhone students.
- **Draw-queue rendering** to the canvas (rectangles, circles, ellipses, lines, text), plus two ordering/correctness fixes (create events now fire before the first step; the object-side-file merge).

**Proof:** a Playwright harness drove both games in headless Chromium — maze_1 boots and the player moves with arrow keys; match3_1 loads the Python runtime, renders the board (pixel-verified), and plays a full click-swap → flash → slide → score round through real mouse events, zero console errors. 14/14 checks. The suite is at 1479 passed, 0 failed with a new regression file pinning all of it.

**One caveat for the school network:** a Python-using game loads Pyodide from a CDN, so it needs internet the first time it's opened. If the school network is locked down, there's an "offline bundle" option sketched in TODO.md we can build. And for the classroom summary: **Linux lab → Android APKs and web builds; Mac lab → iPhone builds; any browser → everything.** All three mobile routes for the match-3 sample are now verified or documented.

## [2026-07-10] User

> How come all that was not identified when you ran a complete audit of the PyGameMaker IDE?

### Claude

It's a fair question, and the honest answer is: **the audits and this week's work were looking for two different kinds of problems, and each is nearly blind to the other's.**

**The audits were static code review.** Eighteen finder agents read existing code hunting for defects — logic errors, crashes, leaks, security holes — and every finding was adversarially verified *against the code*. That method found 111 real bugs, including a dozen export bugs (the Kivy conditional-scoping and keyboard-release fixes, the broken Mobile dialog, the export dialog that no-op'd in French...). But this week's showstoppers weren't defective code — they were **absent code**: an event map with no `mouse_*` entries, a scene loop where step 8 was a comment, an action switch with no `case 'start_moving_direction'`. To a reviewer reading `engine.js`, that file looks coherent and correct — it correctly handles everything it handles. "This engine is missing features that the shipped games actually use" is a claim about the *relationship* between two artifacts, and no finder was asked to cross-reference them.

**Some of it was invisible to any static method.** The Android failure was environmental and temporal: a 40-minute timeout looks perfectly generous in code review; it's only wrong when a real first build takes 75 minutes on real hardware. Buildozer silently skipping hidden directories only bites when you actually run buildozer against a dotted path. These are only findable by doing what we did this week — running the pipeline to completion.

**The samples never exercised these paths — until three days ago.** Every previously bundled sample is a sprite+keyboard game imported from GameMaker. The audits ran against a world where no shipped content used draw-queue rendering or mouse input, so no test, no smoke run, and no user report ever lit up those exporter paths. match3_1 is the *first* mouse-driven, draw-queue game to ship — the moment it became a sample, it created coverage demands that had never existed. (Even the maze_1 HTML5 breakage stayed hidden because nothing ever *played* an HTML5 export; the tests checked that files were generated.)

**What actually found these bugs was a different instrument: a concrete artifact goal.** "Produce a working APK of this game" forces the entire path — export, build, install-shape, input, rendering — and any absence anywhere fails loudly. That's verification against a spec, not review of code.

The durable lesson isn't "audit harder," it's "keep both instruments." Audits find bugs in code that exists; goal-driven end-to-end runs find integrations that don't. This week left behind the missing second instrument: the stub-Kivy test that *plays* the exported match3 round, the Playwright test that clicks the HTML5 export in a real browser, the persistent-build APK pipeline that's now been run to completion. I've also saved this as a working note for future sessions, with the cheap systematic follow-up spelled out: a matrix test cross-referencing *features used by each bundled sample* against *features implemented by each export target*, so the next match3_2-style sample can't silently outgrow an exporter again. Want me to build that matrix test next?

---

## [2026-07-10] User

> continue

### Claude

Picking up where I left off: the plateforme_1 data revealed the deeper bug — the HTML5 engine's sequential conditional logic treats *everything* until the next `if_`/`else` as the guarded branch, while GameMaker semantics (and the IDE runtime) guard only the **next action or block**. That's why the ball's gravity/vspeed logic misbehaves. Let me check exactly how the IDE runtime scopes this, so I mirror it:

All 23 browser checks green across four samples. Now recompute the registries — first the extractor needs to see `draw_score` (handled in `onDraw`, not a switch case):

The drift detection works exactly as designed — all STALE, zero NEW. Pruning the registries and adding the now-supported `destroy` event:

Suite fully green (1485 passed, 0 failed). Updating TODO.md and committing:

Done — pushed as `089fa1a`. Two more samples are now fully web-exportable, and the fix turned out to be much bigger than the registry entries suggested.

**The headline discovery:** while implementing plateforme_1's two "missing actions," the browser verification exposed that the HTML5 engine's *conditional execution model itself* was wrong for every game. GameMaker semantics (and our IDE runtime) say a question action guards only the **next action or block** — the engine instead treated everything up to the next `if`/`else` as the branch. So then-branches over-ran, and everything after an `else` ran unconditionally. That's why plateforme_1's ball fell through the floor with runaway speed. I replaced the whole mechanism with the runtime's exact flat skip-next semantics, and fixed three more latent bugs the rewrite surfaced: `exit_event` only exited one action instead of the event, nested then/else branches were silently dropped by an empty stub, and plateforme rooms crashed the loader outright because their instances use the legacy `object` key (all 120 instances of plateforme_1 — that export had **never booted**).

**Verified in headless Chromium, 23/23 checks:** maze_2 boots clean, renders its score display, and destroying a diamond awards +10 through the newly-fired `destroy` event; plateforme_1 boots clean and the ball settles onto the ground at vspeed 0 — gravity gate, `move_to_contact` landing, and the `test_variable` speed cap all live; maze_1 and match3_1 re-verified green after the rewrite.

**Matrix status:** maze_2 and plateforme_1 join maze_1 and match3_1 as fully covered — the registry pruned itself under test enforcement (the drift check flagged exactly the 25 now-stale entries and zero new ones, which is the mechanism working as designed). Remaining for full web parity: maze_3 and plateforme_2–5, needing 1–10 actions each (`set_sprite`, `play_sound`, the `draw_*` family, `create_moving/random_instance`, …) plus four event types. plateforme_2 is the smallest bite left — a single action (`set_sprite`). Suite: 1485 passed, 0 failed.

Say "next step" again and I'll keep burning down the registry — plateforme_2 and plateforme_3 together need only five actions, though `play_sound` means building audio support into the HTML5 export (sounds aren't even embedded in the page yet), which is the one genuinely new subsystem in the remainder.

## [2026-07-12] User

> Would it make sense to place into extensions the different fuctions to create executables for linux, for windows, for Mac OS or .APK?

### Claude

Good architectural question — and the honest answer is: **as an internal plug-in registry, yes; as separately-installed extensions, not yet.** Let me split those, because they're different levels of "extension":

## What would pay off now: an exporter registry inside the IDE

The exporters are already well-isolated (`export/exe/`, `export/linux/`, `export/macos/`, `export/android/`, `export/HTML5/`, `export/Kivy/`), but the *wiring* is hardcoded: `ide_window.export_game()` builds six radio buttons by hand, each with its own platform-availability logic, and dispatches on numeric IDs. That's why we found a whole dead second export dialog nobody noticed, and why the Android option was invisible enough that you asked me where it was.

A light registry would fix that: each exporter module registers itself with an id, a display label, an availability probe ("needs WSL", "needs macOS + Xcode"), and a run entry point; the Export dialog and the CLI/tests build themselves from the registry. Adding or dropping a target becomes a one-file change, the availability messages live next to the exporter they describe, and the feature-matrix test can iterate the registry instead of a hardcoded list. This is "extension-shaped" architecture with none of the packaging cost — a contained refactor I'd happily do.

## What I'd hold off on: true downloadable/separate extensions

Three reasons, specific to this project:

1. **The heavy parts aren't in the exporters.** The exporters themselves are small pure-Python code generators; the actual weight (buildozer + Android SDK, WSL, Xcode, PyInstaller) is *external tooling that's already not bundled*. Splitting the exporters into installable extensions would slim the IDE download by almost nothing, while adding real infrastructure: plugin discovery, per-extension packaging and CI, version-compatibility handling — and plugin loading inside a PyInstaller-frozen app is its own can of worms.

2. **Version coupling is a feature here, not a bug.** The exporters embed the runtime's semantics — that's exactly what this month's work was about, and the parity guarantee is enforced by tests that live *in the same repo* as the runtime and the samples (`test_export_feature_matrix.py`, the compile gates, the browser harnesses). Separately-versioned exporter extensions could drift from the runtime between releases, which would re-open precisely the class of silent breakage we just closed.

3. **They share internals.** Android and iOS are both thin wrappers around the Kivy exporter; the three desktop targets share PyInstaller scaffolding. Cutting extension boundaries through that graph either duplicates the shared layers or forces an awkward "extension that depends on another extension" story.

There's also the timing argument from your own project's playbook: stability over structure. This code was just hardened with a large verified test surface; a structural migration now churns exactly the files that were just stabilized, for a benefit that's mostly organizational.

## Where full extensions *would* make sense later

There's already a precedent queued up: the Thymio/Open Roberta tooling is hidden for 1.0 and planned as a post-1.0 extension (`docs/POST_1_0_REFACTOR.md`), and there's even an embryonic plugin mechanism in `events/plugin_loader.py`. If and when that Thymio extension work happens, it will force the real questions (how plugins are discovered, packaged, version-checked in a frozen app) — and *then* moving exporters onto the same mechanism becomes cheap and consistent, rather than inventing a plugin system for exporters first. A concrete trigger would also be classroom-driven: if you ever want lab machines with only HTML5 export visible, that's registry-level configuration (hide targets), not extension-level packaging.

**My recommendation:** do the registry refactor (internal, low-risk, kills the dead dialog, makes the Export UI honest and data-driven), and defer installable extensions until the Thymio work defines the plugin story. Want me to do the registry pass?

## [2026-07-12] User

> what does this mean:
> L9 now drives the real export_windows_exe shell with a stub IDE and asserts the settings that arrive at ExeExporter

### Claude

That sentence describes *how the regression test works* after the refactor. Let me unpack it piece by piece, with the actual code.

**What L9 is.** In the 2026-06-11 audit, finding L9 was: "the Export Options checkboxes do nothing — the code ignores what the user ticked and always sends a hardcoded `{include_assets: True, optimize: True, include_debug: False}` to the exporter." It was fixed, and a test was written to make sure the bug can never quietly come back. When I deleted the old dialog yesterday, that test would have died with it — so it had to be rebuilt against the new code path. The sentence describes the rebuilt test.

**"Drives the real `export_windows_exe` shell".** `export_windows_exe` is the actual production method in the IDE that runs when you pick "Windows Executable" and click Export. The test calls *that real method* — not a copy of its logic, not a simplified imitation. This matters because a test that re-implements the logic it's testing proves nothing; this one executes the same code your students' clicks execute.

**"With a stub IDE."** Normally that method lives on the full IDE window — menus, editors, a Qt event loop, an open project. Booting all of that in a test is slow and brittle. So the test hands the method a *stand-in object* that provides only the five things the method actually touches, with the interactive parts replaced by canned answers:

```python
class StubIDE:
    _export_options = {"include_assets": False, "optimize": False, "include_debug": True}

    def _require_open_project(self):      # pretend a project is open
        return True
    def _ask_export_dir(self, suffix):    # pretend the user picked a folder
        return "/tmp/out"
    def _run_export_with_progress(self, **kwargs):
        captured.update(kwargs["export_settings"])   # ← the trap

PyGameMakerIDE.export_windows_exe(StubIDE())
```

So: real export method, fake surroundings. No windows open, no PyInstaller runs, no user needed — the test finishes in milliseconds.

**"Asserts the settings that arrive at ExeExporter."** The stub's `_run_export_with_progress` is the last stop before the settings would be handed to the real exporter — instead of exporting, it just *records* what it was given. Then the test checks the recording:

```python
assert settings["include_debug"] is True     # the user ticked it → it must arrive
assert settings["optimize"] is False         # the user unticked it → that must arrive too
```

If anyone ever reintroduces the L9 bug — hardcoding the options again anywhere between the dialog and the exporter — the recorded settings won't match what the "user" chose, and the test fails on the spot, naming exactly which option got swallowed.

In one sentence: **the test plays a pretend user who ticks unusual checkboxes, pushes those choices through the genuine export code with all the GUI machinery faked out, and verifies the choices survive the whole journey to the exporter's doorstep.** It's the same philosophy as the browser and stub-kivy tests from the export work: test the real code path end-to-end, fake only the environment around it.

## [2026-07-12] User

> would it mnake sense do run a full audit now?

### Claude

Yes — but scoped, not full. Here's my honest read:

## Why not a *full* re-audit

The June full audit (18 finders, adversarially verified, 111 findings) covered the whole codebase, and all 111 were closed with regression tests. Outside the export subsystem, the code has barely moved since — re-auditing it would mostly re-derive the five batches of already-rejected findings that CLAUDE.md warns about (the `eval` gating, the "unbounded" caches, the file-size complaints…). History in this repo shows re-audits of stable code produce noise that costs verification effort.

## Why a scoped audit of the July delta makes real sense

Since June 11, roughly three thousand lines of new or rewritten code landed, and **none of it has ever been adversarially reviewed** — it was all verified the *other* way (end-to-end: browser harnesses, stub-kivy round tests, a real APK). Your own project memory states the principle: audits and end-to-end verification catch disjoint bug classes. July proved e2e catches what audits miss; the inverse is still due. The un-audited surface:

- **`engine.js` (~2,600 lines of JavaScript)** — the biggest item. The June audits were Python-focused; the HTML5 engine has *never* been audited, and we already know its baseline quality was poor (the conditional model was structurally wrong, exit_event was broken, plateforme instances crashed the loader). I fixed what the samples exercised — an audit would review what they don't: the pre-existing collision/bounce/grid internals, error paths, and at least one lead I'd flag myself: the engine `eval()`s expression strings from project data in the browser (`if_collision` offsets), which deserves the same scrutiny the Python `eval` got in June.
- **The Kivy exporter templates and code generator** after two parity batches, including generated-code edge cases beyond what the compile gate proves.
- **The Android exporter / WSL bridge** rewrite (persistent dirs, timeouts, the build script).
- **The Pyodide bridge** (JS↔Python state sync, error paths when Pyodide fails mid-game).
- Smaller: `export/registry.py`, the consolidation changes, the match3_1 game code.

## Timing is right

1.1.0 just shipped and the classroom deployment is ahead. An audit now means fixes land calmly in a 1.1.1 before students touch it, rather than as hotfixes during a lesson. And the new export test infrastructure (matrix, compile gates, browser harnesses) means every audit fix can be verified far more cheaply than in June.

## How I'd run it

Per the established methodology: ~6–8 finders scoped to `export/` + `runtime`-touching July diffs, with the known-rejected list baked into the prompts, adversarial verification of every finding before it enters the registry, then the usual burn-down — one fix per commit, each with a regression test, checkboxes in a `docs/` registry. Given the June experience, I'd expect a moderate haul: most findings in `engine.js`'s pre-existing internals, a handful in the templates, and a few overstated ones to reject.

Realistic expectation to set: this is a token-heavy multi-agent run (the June one was the largest workflow this repo has seen), and findings are leads, not ground truth — the verification pass afterward is where the real time goes.

**My recommendation: yes, run it, scoped to the export subsystem with `engine.js` as the priority target.** Say the word and I'll launch the audit workflow.

> What is best? I just purchased some usage credits but I am still blocked

### Claude

A couple of things are worth separating, because "I bought credits but I'm still blocked" usually means the credits aren't the thing being limited.

**Why credits may not unblock you immediately:**
- The error was a **session/usage-window limit** ("resets 12:50pm Zurich") — that's a *time-based* cap, not a *credit-balance* cap. Buying credits tops up a balance; it doesn't always lift a rolling-window rate limit. If that's what you hit, the block clears at **12:50 Zurich** regardless of credits.
- New credit balances often need a **client restart to pick up** — quitting and reopening the Claude Code extension / VS Code (or starting a fresh session) forces it to re-read your entitlement rather than the cached limited state.
- There can be a few minutes of **propagation delay** after a purchase.

I can't see your billing state from inside the session, so I can't tell you definitively which of those it is — but the fastest diagnostic is: **restart the client and try one small action.** If it still says "resets 12:50," it's the time window and you wait; if it works, it was a cached-balance issue.

**What's best to actually do with the audit** — regardless of when you're unblocked, don't re-run the full 8-finder version. It's what exhausted the window in the first place (~490k tokens, all high-effort). The best-value plan:

1. **Run the `engine.js`-only slice first, at medium effort** — 4 finders on the priority target (the never-audited JS engine), roughly a third of the cost. That's where the real risk is anyway.
2. **Kivy / Android / registry finders as a separate later pass** — those files are smaller and lower-risk, and splitting them keeps any single run well under the limit.

Staged like that, each run is cheap enough that a topped-up balance comfortably covers it, and you're not gambling the whole audit on one big workflow again.

## The audit worked — and found a real, live bug

**5 confirmed / 14 rejected** from 19 raw candidates. The 14 rejections mean the adversarial pass did its job (it wasn't rubber-stamping). The synthesis step and some verify votes died on the session limit again (resets 17:50 Zurich), but the finding phase and most verification completed.

**The headline (H1) I personally verified against the real code, and it's live:** a cluster of GameMaker "question" actions — `test_expression`, `check_empty`, `check_collision` — aren't recognized as conditionals by the HTML5 engine. Two consequences: they never gate the action they're supposed to guard (it runs unconditionally), and they exist only as literal do-nothing stubs. **The bundled samples use `test_expression` 10× and `check_empty` 6×**, so their web exports have broken conditional logic *today*. This is exactly the class of defect the export matrix and browser harnesses couldn't catch — they verify that actions *export*, not that unexercised conditional *branches evaluate correctly*. That's the audit earning its keep.

The other four: one medium I find plausible (collision tests ignore sprite origin, while `getBoundingBox` applies it — a real inconsistency), two that fold into H1, and one low (unescaped project name in the HTML). I've marked those "pending personal re-verification" — I'll confirm each against code before any fix, per the repo's leads-not-ground-truth rule.

## What was fixed

**H1 (high, live) — `5f38841`.** The headline: `test_expression`, `check_empty`, `check_collision` (plus `test_lives/health/chance/question`) weren't recognized as conditionals and were literal no-op stubs, so their guarded branches ran unconditionally. **M1 (medium) — `96fb7b5`.** The `if_collision` condition built its probe box from raw positions, ignoring sprite origin, while the main collision path applied it — so centered-origin sprites mis-detected. Routed it through the same origin-aware helper. **3/3 browser checks** with a centered-origin sprite the old code would have missed.

**L1 (low) — `e71f9a7`.** Project name interpolated unescaped into the HTML — now `html.escape`d. The fix **surfaced a worse related bug**: the name was also the output *filename*, so a perfectly reasonable name like `Level 1: Go` crashed the export on Windows (illegal `:`). Added filename sanitization too.

So, concretely — pick one:
- **"cut 1.1.1"** → I bump the version, write the CHANGELOG, and tag/push (binaries build on GitHub).
- **"verify on device first"** → hold the release; you install the match3 APK / open a web export on real hardware, report back, then we tag.

## [2026-07-14] User

> We need to tailor the work so that each task fits in the session limit.

### Claude

Agreed — and this session gave us the data to do it precisely. The pattern was unambiguous: **fan-out blew the limit; sequential work didn't.**

- The multi-agent audit **Workflows** (15–28 parallel agents, ~500k–860k tokens each) hit the limit **three times**, always mid-run.
- The **single-agent finders** (one agent, ~50–75k tokens) both completed cleanly.
- **Main-thread work** — reading, editing, testing, committing — kept working even while subagents were blocked.

So the rule isn't "do less," it's "**don't fan out, and make every unit independently shippable.**"

## The operating model

1. **One task ≈ one commit.** A fix + its regression test + verification = one unit (exactly the H1/M1/L1/KA/KCG/EIO cadence). Never bundle "audit everything" into one task.
2. **No multi-agent Workflows on this account.** If parallel investigation is needed, run **one Agent at a time** and verify between them — which is what finally got the Kivy/Android audit done.
3. **Verify on the main thread, not with subagents.** Reading code to confirm a finding is cheap and never blocked; spawning verifier fan-out is what died. (I confirmed every audit finding by hand this way.)
4. **Front-load the durable artifact and commit/push after each unit.** Write the plan/registry to a file *first* so a partial session still leaves a usable record; push after every fix so a mid-session limit loses nothing and the next session resumes from clean `main`. The registry checkboxes are the resume state.
5. **The todo list is the unit tracker** — one entry per commit-sized task, so scope stays visible and a session can stop cleanly at any boundary.

Rough sizing heuristic from what we saw: a single fix-unit or one focused finder is comfortably within budget; anything that fans out to a dozen-plus agents is over. When a job is inherently big (a full audit), **decompose it into a queue of finder-then-fix units** and work the queue one at a time — never launch the whole thing as one workflow.

This is a standing preference about *how* I should work, so it belongs in durable memory rather than just this conversation. Let me record it:

Recorded — so future sessions start with this as a default, not a lesson to relearn.

Two things to close the loop:

**This is now my default here.** Going forward I'll decompose any large ask into a queue of commit-sized units, work them one at a time, verify by reading rather than fanning out, and commit+push after each — so you never lose progress to a mid-session limit, and any session can stop cleanly at a unit boundary.

**One thing you can do on your side to make tasks fit better:** give me the *goal* and let me propose the decomposition before I start. When you say "audit the export subsystem," the right move (which we arrived at the hard way) is for me to first write the plan/registry, commit it, then work finder→verify→fix one unit at a time — rather than launching one big thing. If I ever reach for a multi-agent Workflow again on a big job, tell me to break it up; that's the anti-pattern this is guarding against.

Optionally, I can also drop a short version of this into the repo's `CLAUDE.md` (the in-repo working notes) so it travels with the project across machines and to any other contributor's Claude sessions — the memory I just wrote is machine-local to this box. Want me to add that in-repo note too?

- Kivy runs in **y-up** space (instances are created with a `y_kivy = room_height − y_gm − h` flip), so the camera transform's y-axis has to be worked out carefully — the kind of thing that goes wrong quietly.
- The views config has to be **baked into the generated scene template** (unlike HTML5, where it's embedded data).
- It needs a **canvas-transform restructure** (PushMatrix/Translate around the scene, composing with the existing Android scaling container).
- Full multi-view/port parity needs **StencilView** clipping — a real lift beyond single-view.

Starting that with the budget I had left risked leaving it half-done, which our rule says to avoid. So instead I did the small, complete registration unit, and left Kivy as its own clean unit for a fresh session.

**Honest status, now recorded in TODO.md and the plan:** views work on **desktop ✅ and HTML5 ✅**, Kivy/Android **❌ pending**. The registration is truthful — the action genuinely works on 2 of 3 targets — but a Kivy export of a views game won't scroll until that unit lands.

The plan ([docs/VIEWS_SAMPLES_PLAN.md](docs/VIEWS_SAMPLES_PLAN.md)) has the progress note, so the next session resumes cold on any machine. Clean stopping point: **next unit is the Kivy camera** (its own dedicated budget), then the 3-target parity test, then the `views_1`/`views_2` samples.

## [2026-07-15] User

> Just answer do not work on it
> Is the documentation on the samples available when PyGamemaker is executed from a compiled executable?

### Claude

I'll check the build spec and how samples are surfaced — reading only, not changing anything.

Short answer: **the files are bundled, but nothing in the app displays them.**

Details, from the build spec and UI code:

- **Physically bundled — yes.** `PyGameMaker.spec` packs the entire `samples/` tree into the frozen executable via `Tree('samples', prefix='samples')` ([PyGameMaker.spec:159](PyGameMaker.spec#L159)). `Tree` includes *every* file in the folder, so each sample's `README.md` and `CREDITS.txt` (including the new `samples/views_1/README.md`) are inside the compiled app.

- **Surfaced in the UI — no.** There is no README viewer anywhere. The Welcome tab ([widgets/welcome_tab.py](widgets/welcome_tab.py)) only lists samples and opens the *project* — it never reads or renders the README. A repo-wide grep for `README` in `widgets/`, `editors/`, `ui/`, `main.py` finds nothing that displays a sample README (only an unrelated Blockly README).

So the practical situation for an end user of a compiled build:

- The docs exist on disk but are **effectively hidden**. In a **single-file** PyInstaller build they're extracted to an ephemeral `_MEIPASS` temp dir at launch and deleted on exit — very hard to find. In a **one-folder** build they sit under `_internal/samples/<name>/README.md`, findable only by manually browsing the install directory.
- There's no "View documentation / About this sample" affordance in the app, so a student/teacher running the `.exe` would not naturally encounter the READMEs at all. The spec comment even notes samples are "read-only at runtime — users must Save As to a writable location before editing," reinforcing that the folder isn't meant to be browsed in place.

If you ever want that documentation to actually reach users of the compiled build, it'd need a small UI addition (e.g., a "Docs" button on each Welcome-tab sample card that loads the bundled `README.md` into a text/markdown view) — but that's a change, not something present today.

---

## [2026-07-15] User

> Make a plan to implement the additional"Docs" button for each sample and tell me if we can do it now. We are at 67% usage in this session

### Claude

I have what I need to plan concretely. Here's the plan and my go/no-go.

## Key constraint I found

Samples are **not** individual cards — they're collapsed into a **single dropdown** ("Choose a sample"), each an `(label, callback)` menu action ([welcome_tab.py:188](widgets/welcome_tab.py#L188)). This was a deliberate UX call (comment at [:168](widgets/welcome_tab.py#L168): "five vertical buttons made the Welcome tab feel overstuffed"). So there's no per-card surface to bolt a button onto, and re-expanding samples into cards would reverse that decision. The plan therefore adapts "a Docs button per sample" to fit the dropdown design.

## Recommended design

A **"📖 Sample guides" dialog** — one new button under the samples dropdown that opens a small modal: sample list on the left, the selected sample's rendered `README.md` on the right. This keeps the one-click "open project" path untouched, gives per-sample docs access, and scales as samples grow (better than a second parallel dropdown of docs).

## Implementation steps

1. **`SampleDocsDialog`** (new small class, in `widgets/` — either its own file or inside `welcome_tab.py`): a `QDialog` with a `QListWidget` (populated from the same `SAMPLE_PROJECTS` that survive the `project.json` existence check) and a `QTextBrowser`. On selection, load `samples/<name>/README.md` and render via `QTextBrowser.setMarkdown()` (PySide6 6.10 supports it; fall back to `setPlainText` if empty). `setOpenExternalLinks(True)` for links; missing/unreadable README → a friendly "No documentation for this sample" placeholder.
2. **Path resolution** reuses `_repo_root()` — the same root the dropdown already uses to find samples, so it works identically in the frozen build (READMEs are bundled, confirmed in `PyGameMaker.spec:159`).
3. **Wire a button** in `_build_left_column`, right after the samples dropdown: `self.tr("📖  Sample guides")` → opens the dialog. ~10 lines.
4. **Test** (`tests/test_sample_docs_dialog.py`): offscreen `qapp`, construct the dialog, assert it lists the samples, loads `views_1`'s README text, and shows the placeholder for a bogus sample. Uses the `pytest-qt` path (CI runs it).
5. **Translation** of the one new button label — defer to a quick follow-up (or fold into the next translation run); English shows fine meanwhile.

**Files touched:** `widgets/welcome_tab.py` (+ maybe one new widget file), `tests/test_sample_docs_dialog.py`. No engine/runtime changes. One commit.

## Can we do it now at 67%?

**Yes, but it's near the upper edge of what the remaining ~33% comfortably fits** — and it's riskier than the translations unit for two reasons: it's GUI code (needs `pytest-qt` and a bit of behavior iteration I can't visually verify from here), and GUI polish sometimes needs a second pass. It's a clean single-commit unit, so if it overruns we stop at the commit boundary and lose nothing.

**My recommendation:** doable now **if scoped tightly** — viewer dialog + button + test in one commit, defer the button-label translation. If you'd rather not risk a mid-unit limit, this is also a perfectly good first unit for a fresh full-budget session (it's self-contained and needs no prior context).

Want me to proceed now with that tight scope, or queue it for next session?

## [2026-07-15] User

> start on the Kivy multi-view unit now

### Claude

Let me investigate the current Kivy render architecture before committing to a design — multi-view in a retained-mode widget system is the crux. Let me re-read the scene structure I built and how instances render.

Let me check how the window/game size is set — critical for whether the Kivy camera even clips to a viewport:

This is a critical finding. Let me read the full window-config block to confirm how the window is sized on desktop vs Android:

I've hit a material discovery that changes what this unit should be. Let me verify one detail before I lay it out:

Before writing any code, I need to surface a significant finding — it changes what this unit should be.

## What I found

Investigating the Kivy render path to add multi-view, I discovered the **single-view Kivy camera is itself incomplete**, and the reason cuts to the heart of the multi-view work:

- **The Kivy export sizes its window to the whole room, not the view.** [kivy_exporter.py:312](export/Kivy/kivy_exporter.py#L312) sets `GAME_WIDTH = room_width` (2400 for views_1) and [:385](export/Kivy/kivy_exporter.py#L385) does `Window.size = (GAME_WIDTH, GAME_HEIGHT)`. The exporter **never reads** the project's `window_width`/`window_height` (800×600). It works for maze/plateforme/match3 only because their room == window.
- So my earlier single-view camera computes the *right* `view_x` and translates the scene — but inside a **2400-wide window**, so you'd see the whole room with content sliding sideways into empty space, not an 800×600 scrolling viewport. The `test_kivy_views` test only verified the follow *math* with stubs, never the actual windowed display. That's the "audits miss absent features — every export target needs an end-to-end play test" trap.

- **True multi-view (N ports, e.g. a minimap) is a big, GL-bound change.** Kivy instances are retained-mode child widgets, so rendering the scene into two ports requires an **FBO** (render the room to a texture once, blit a textured quad per port). That reworks the Kivy render model that *every* sample depends on, and the FBO capture can't be exercised headlessly on this box (no GL context) — only its instruction structure could be stub-tested.

## The fork

Given single-view is broken and multi-view needs a risky GL rewrite, "just add multi-view" isn't the right next step. Let me get your steer:

The user wants the full FBO multi-view. This is the big architectural unit — I'll implement it carefully, branching on `views_enabled` so non-views samples stay byte-identical (zero regression risk). Let me study how instances render their sprite and bind to `pos`, since the FBO must capture their canvases.

This unit turned out bigger than "add multi-view" because investigation uncovered that the single-view Kivy camera was itself broken. What landed:

**The foundational bug fix:** the Kivy export was sizing the OS window to the *room* (2400 wide), so the camera scrolled content inside a room-sized window instead of showing an 800×600 slice. `GAME_WIDTH` is now `800` for views_1 (was 2400). My earlier stub test only checked the follow *math*, never the windowed display — exactly the "audits miss absent features" trap in your memory.

**The multi-view render (what you chose):**
- The whole room renders once into an **Fbo** (offscreen texture); instance canvases attach to it instead of being child widgets.
- `_render_views()` blits **each visible view's** room region into its screen port via `tex_coords` — so a main view + a fixed minimap show the same room at once. A port defaults to view size (1:1).
- `update_views()` now follows **every** view; fixed views (a minimap) hold position.
- Touch→room maps through the main view's camera offset.

**Safety:** every branch guards on `views_enabled`, so maze/plateforme/match3 keep the original child-widget path byte-for-byte.

So a zoomed-out minimap (view ≫ port) would look different on each target. For a sample honestly verifiable on all 3, both views must be **1:1** (view size == port size).

That makes **split-screen** the right design (the plan's alternative to a minimap): two equal 1:1 viewports, split **left/right** so `port_y=0`/full-height avoids the Kivy y-up vs desktop/HTML5 y-down port flip. Let me build `views_2` as split-screen co-op.

Two real design problems I found and solved along the way:

1. **Desktop/HTML5 don't scale views to ports** (they clip + offset 1:1); only my new Kivy FBO scales. So a zoomed-out minimap would render differently per target. I made both views **1:1** and split **left/right** (so `port_y=0`/full-height also dodges the Kivy y-up vs desktop/HTML5 y-down port flip) — renders identically everywhere.

2. **Global key-state would freeze one player while the other holds a key** (`nokey` only fires when *both* release everything). I verified the runtime dispatches `keyboard_release` **per-key/per-object**, then gave each player `keyboard_release` stops for its own keys — so the two are truly independent.

**Verified on all three targets:** desktop deep-smoke test proves the two cameras scroll **independently** and clamp; HTML5 + Kivy exports carry the two-view config (HTML5 right-half `port_x=400`, Kivy window sized to 800); smoke runner passes (284 instances). Registered in the Welcome tab, documented (README explains the `keyboard_release` and 1:1/no-minimap rationale), progression narrative extended. Suite **1638 → 1643 passed**.

This exercises the Kivy FBO multi-view I built earlier — a real multi-viewport game now runs through that path. Clean commit boundary; tree is clean.

**Remaining follow-ups** (both small, queued): translate the "Views — Level 2" Welcome-tab label, and seed `random` in the pre-existing match3_2 headless flake. Good place to pause.

**What you found:** GM's **"Applies to"** selector was never imported — at all, for any action, in any `.gmk`. Two stacked bugs:

- `change_instance` ×2 → **all scared→monster** (reset), then **all monster→scared**,
- eating a scared monster reverts **it** (target other).

1805 passed (+6), 0 failed. Update the maze_4 checklist with the findings ledger, refresh the build, and commit:

Big one found and fixed — pushed (`06d1051`). Your maze_4 report cracked open a **core engine bug** that affects every imported GM game:

**The "moves right, can't stop" mystery:** obj_person's step event is four conveyor chains (`test_alignment → if_collision(move_marker) → move`). GM's rule is that a false question skips the next question *and everything it guards*, recursively — but our executor skipped exactly **one** action. So on any misaligned frame, all four movement actions ran unconditionally and the last one (right, speed 8) won every frame, steamrolling your arrow keys. I reproduced it headlessly — hspeed jumped 4→8 one frame after pressing RIGHT, in a room with **zero** movement markers — then fixed both action-list executors and re-verified: steady speed-4 movement, clean aligned stop at the wall. 6 new tests pin the exact chain shape. This bug would have corrupted *any* imported game using chained conditionals — likely another chunk of the original "bad action parameters" verdict.

**Your build is refreshed** — movement should now feel right.

**Three findings from your report remain open, all diagnosed** (in the checklist so nothing's lost):
- **#6 — the "will not move" residue:** pressing a key *toward* a flush wall slides you ~3px into it (pygm2 slides-to-contact; GM8 reverts to the pre-move position), knocking you off the 32-grid so the alignment gate wedges you permanently. I verified this headlessly (UP against a wall → y=413 → dead controls). This is next — it needs either a careful engine decision or a maze_1-style `snap_to_grid` hand-patch at re-add.

- **The render loop** subsurfaces a 1px vertical strip and scales it to the projected wall height, keeping the half-brightness away-face depth cue (`BLEND_RGB_MULT`).
- **A `wall_texture` config param** (sprite name) textures every wall with one image — necessary because the maze's derived collision sprites are thin black edge-placeholders, not real wall art. Resolution order: `wall_texture` → per-instance sprite → flat color; `wall_textured: false` forces the old look. Both plumbed through `enable_raycast_view`.
- **`raycast_1`** ships a generated 64×64 tileable **brick texture** and uses it — I verified the result by rendering PNG frames (the corridor shot above: brick walls receding with correct perspective and side shading, sky ceiling, floor, brick wall capping the end).

**Backward compatible:** texturing is sprite-gated, so spriteless walls and all existing flat-color pixel tests are untouched. 6 new tests (`TestTexturedWalls`). Suite **1870 passed, 0 failed**.

A design note worth surfacing: the earlier "thin edge-wall" rethink means a wall's *collision* sprite (32×8 / 8×32 black lines) isn't a usable *texture* — that's exactly why I added the `wall_texture` override rather than forcing per-instance sprites. Cleaner separation of collision geometry from wall art.

**Next raycast steps**, per the plan's reprioritized order — each its own session-sized unit:
1. **Sky/horizon (Phase 5b)** — the cheapest (a pan-and-tile strip, no distance math) and very DOOM-like; would need `raycast_1`'s map opened at an edge to actually be visible.
2. **Floor/ceiling textures** — the riskier one (per-pixel floor-casting, flagged for a Phase-0-style timing check first).
3. **HTML5/Kivy export parity** — larger, ports the whole raycast renderer.

Want me to continue with the sky/horizon next (cheapest, high visual payoff), or take a different one?

**Phase 5b — DOOM-style panning sky is done and pushed** (`2a7ac53`). `raycast_1` now has a proper sky overhead.

**What landed:**
- The ceiling region renders a **sky panorama** (when `sky_texture` is set) instead of a flat fill. Classic Doom treatment — the sky is infinitely far, so it **doesn't recede with distance**; it just **pans horizontally with `facing_angle`**. The math: `pano_w = w·360/fov` (the FOV-wide screen shows exactly `fov/360` of the panorama), `pan = (facing_angle % 360)/360 · pano_w`, blitted with a wraparound copy — so a full 360° turn pans the sky exactly once and a landmark returns to the same screen spot.
- **Cheapest texture in the pipeline** (as the plan predicted): one scale + up to two blits, no per-column distance math. Drawn under the walls, so wall strips occlude it for free — and it's visible in the ceiling region above the walls, so `raycast_1`'s enclosed maze shows it fine (no open-edge map needed after all).
- `raycast_1` ships a generated **256×64 tileable sky** (blue gradient + sun + wrap-safe clouds). I verified the gradient/cloud render and confirmed the pan pixel-to-pixel.

**Backward compatible:** absent `sky_texture`, the flat ceiling fill is unchanged, so nothing else is affected. 4 new tests (`TestSky`). Suite **1874 passed, 0 failed**.

The corridor now reads as a genuine outdoor Wolfenstein level: brick walls receding with perspective and side-shading, a gradient sky with drifting clouds overhead that swings as you turn, and a floor below.

**Remaining raycast work** (two units left, in rough order):
1. **Floor/ceiling texture-casting** — the last Phase 5 piece and the *riskiest*: it's per-pixel-row work, flagged in the plan for a Phase-0-style timing check before committing (pure-Python floor casting can be slow; there's a documented low-res fallback).
2. **HTML5/Kivy export parity** — the biggest remaining chunk: porting the whole raycast renderer (walls + sky + billboards) to the two export targets so `raycast_1` isn't desktop-only.

Want me to take on the **floor casting** next (I'd start with a timing spike, per the plan), or the **export parity**? Or pause here — this is a clean stopping point with two visually complete phases shipped.

**This unit — textured floor:**
- The plan **mandated a timing spike first**, and it was decisive: full-res per-pixel floor casting measured **~64 ms/frame** in pure Python (unusable, and no numpy per the codebase's dependency stance), while **low-res cast + upscale** is **~5 ms at 4×**. So I shipped the plan's documented low-res fallback.
- `_cast_floor_plane` renders the ground plane into a downsampled surface then `transform.scale`s it up — Lodev camera-plane method (straight floor lines), sampled in **cell units** so the texture tiles once per grid cell, with the row-distance derived from the wall projection so **floor and wall bases meet seamlessly**. `floor_texture` casts the floor; `ceiling_texture` casts the ceiling (only when no sky claims it); `floor_cast_res` trades sharpness for speed.

## What I built

Two self-contained throwaway prototypes in `spikes/`, both faithful ports of the desktop `_cast_floor_plane` algorithm (camera-plane ray interpolation, `rowDistance = 0.5·h/(y−horizon)`, per-cell texture tiling), defaulting to `raycast_1`'s real dimensions (480×480, 240-px floor region, 32×32 texture):

- **`floor_cast_html5.html`** — open in each target browser, click *Run benchmark*. Times a faithful `ImageData` cast across `res ∈ {1,2,4,8}`, showing a PASS/FAIL table against the 8 ms / 33 ms budgets plus the UA string, with a live-render toggle so you can confirm the cast *looks* right, not just that it's fast.
- **`floor_cast_kivy.py`** — run on a real desktop (`py -3.12 spikes/floor_cast_kivy.py`). It opens a Kivy window to get a GL context, then times **both** a naive Python port and a numpy-vectorized cast including the real `Texture.blit_buffer` upload, prints a table, and quits.
- **`README.md`** — the <8 ms decision rule, how to run each, and the flat-floor fallback if even `res=8` misses budget.

## Validation already done here

I verified the naive cast headlessly (CPU-only, no GL): it reproduces the plan's desktop figures **exactly** — `res=1 ≈ 64 ms` (unusable), `res=4 ≈ 3.5 ms`, `res=8 ≈ 1 ms` — which confirms the port is faithful. The output buffer sizes and cast logic all check out. The HTML script is brace-balanced. The one thing I couldn't verify on this box is the numpy variant (numpy isn't installed here), but the spike guards for that and falls back to timing naive-only.

## What you get from running them

On real hardware the spikes add the actual upload cost (`putImageData`+`drawImage` / `blit_buffer`) on top of the CPU cast. The read-off is simple: **the smallest `res` whose median is comfortably under ~8 ms becomes the export default for units 3b/5b.** If naive is too slow but numpy fits, unit 5b should vectorize; if even `res=8` fails on a target, that target ships the flat `floor_color` fill.

Once you've run them and recorded the numbers in `docs/RAYCAST_2_5D_PLAN.md`, the `spikes/` directory is meant to be deleted — it's not imported anywhere and pytest ignores it. Send me the numbers and I can write the actual floor-casting units against them.

## What this fixed

`start_moving_direction`'s `directions` was a plain `string` param, so the events panel edited it through a single-line text box. A list value — what the GMK importer produces from GM's 3×3 direction bitmask (e.g. `['down','up']` for "move in a random one each step") — had no real widget, so it round-tripped as the stringified repr `"['down','up']"`. The runtime only coped via an explicit `ast.literal_eval` fallback.

## The change

- **New `multi_choice` param type** in `ActionConfigDialog`: renders a set of checkboxes (a 3-column `QGridLayout` for 7–9 choices, so directions get GM's familiar **3×3 picker** with *up* on top), and reads the value back as a **real Python list** of the checked canonical names.
- **`_parse_multi_choice`** normalizes every legacy stored form — real list, stringified list, comma-separated, single name — so old saves still populate the boxes.
- `directions` is now `param_type="multi_choice"` with the 9 GM direction names in reading order and a `["right"]` default.

I verified the whole downstream chain tolerates a list value before changing the type (runtime handles it explicitly; Blockly export, the action-summary formatter, and HTML5/Kivy codegen all `str()`/JSON-serialize fine), so this is behavior-preserving for existing projects and eliminates the stringified round-trip for new edits.

**7 new tests** (`test_action_editor_multi_choice.py`) cover the parser across all legacy forms plus the live widget round-trip. **Suite: 1918 → 1925 passed, 0 failed.** The registry checkbox in `GMK_IMPORTER_HARDENING_PLAN.md` is flipped.

## Investigation outcome

Surveyed what draw actions the two samples actually use:
- **treasure** — no draw actions at all.
- **maze_4** — `draw_lives`, `draw_score`, `draw_text`, `set_draw_color`, `set_draw_font` (the audit's `draw_self`/`draw_sprite_ext` examples aren't used by either sample).

All five are already registered, so there's no *missing*-metadata gap. But comparing each saved action's params against its registered metadata surfaced **two genuine UI-metadata gaps** — the runtime honors the params, but the events panel dropped them on edit (the M11/M60 "opening an action and clicking OK corrupts it" class):

1. **`relative`** on `draw_score`/`draw_text`/`draw_lives` — runtime reads it via `_is_relative()`, registry had no widget. → added a `relative` boolean.
2. **`image`** (GM's sprite-arg name) on `draw_lives` — runtime accepts `sprite` *or* `image`, registry had no alias, so editing dropped the life-icon sprite. → added `aliases=["image"]` (migrates to canonical `sprite` on save).

The fix is purely additive to the metadata — no runtime or saved-file changes, so maze_4's actual gameplay is unchanged. **8 new tests; suite 1925 → 1933, 0 failed.**

## Bonus finding (logged, not fixed)

Following the "distinguish importer bug from metadata gap" instruction, I found a **separate, non-metadata issue**: the GMK importer emits GM's `align` int for `set_draw_font`, but *neither the runtime nor the registry reads it* — both use `halign`/`valign` strings. maze_4's `align:"0"` (GM left) coincidentally matches the runtime's `halign` default, so it's **latent, not breaking**, but any GM center/right alignment is silently ignored on import. That's an importer/runtime param-name mismatch, distinct from UI metadata, so I recorded it in `GMK_IMPORTER_HARDENING_PLAN.md` as a tracked follow-up rather than expanding this commit's scope.

That closes the draw-event item. The `set_draw_font`/`align` importer mismatch is a small, well-scoped next task if you want it (either the importer converts GM 0/1/2 → left/center/right into `halign`, or a runtime `align` fallback) — say the word and I'll take it, or we can stop here.

## What this fixed

The `set_draw_font`/`align` importer/runtime mismatch I discovered during the draw-event investigation. GM's Set-Font action encodes horizontal alignment as a `0/1/2` menu under the key `align`, but **nothing downstream read it** — the runtime and the events-panel metadata both use `halign`/`valign` strings. So any GM center/right font alignment was silently dropped on import. maze_4's `align:"0"` (left) coincidentally matched the runtime's default, which is exactly why the bug stayed hidden.

Fixed at the root **and** defensively — three layers so every case is covered:

1. **Importer** (`gmk_converter._convert_font_align`) — maps GM `0/1/2` → `left/center/right` and emits canonical `halign`, dropping the raw `align`. New imports are correct *and* panel-editable.
2. **Runtime fallback** (`execute_set_draw_font_action`) — reads a raw `align` as a `halign` fallback, so **projects imported before this fix render correctly with no re-import**.
3. **Sample hygiene** — normalized maze_4's shipped data (`align:"0"` → `halign:"left"`) in both the object file and the embedded `project.json`.

**6 new tests** (`test_gmk_font_align.py`) — the mapping, a real `_convert_single_action` round-trip (GM `align=1` → `halign=center`, no leftover `align`), the runtime fallback (legacy honored, explicit `halign` wins, canonical still works), and the normalized sample. **Suite: 1933 → 1939 passed, 0 failed.**

## What happened this round

I ran the owed `exporter-io-registry` finder as a single Agent (per the session-limit rule) and verified every lead against the actual code before touching anything. It returned 3 findings; I confirmed all 3 and fixed 2, deferring 1:

**Fixed — PyInstaller `.spec` escaping (EIO-9, EIO-10):** The exe/linux/macos exporters build a `.spec` file (which PyInstaller `exec()`s) by interpolating asset paths and the icon path into string literals — unescaped. Two real crash bugs:
- A French asset filename with an apostrophe (`épée d'or.png`) closed the literal early → `SyntaxError` → **whole export fails**.
- A Windows icon path (`C:\Users\…\icon.ico`) injected an invalid `\U` escape → same crash.

Fixed all sites with forward-slash + `repr()`. The pre-existing `game_name` sanitization proved the author knew this hazard class — `datas`/`icon` were just missed.

**The regression test earned its keep:** by compiling the *full* generated spec for all three exporters, it caught an **additional** unescaped icon interpolation in the macos exporter that the finder's report had missed. That's exactly why the "verify on the main thread" discipline matters — the finder is a lead, not ground truth, in both directions.

**Deferred (EIO-11, low):** Kivy copies assets keyed by basename, so two assets sharing a basename across source dirs silently clobber each other on disk. Real but low-severity, and the fix must re-key the name→path/metadata maps too — logged as a focused follow-up rather than bundled into a clean escaping fix.

## What this fixed

EIO-11 — the last open export-audit item. The Kivy exporter copied every sprite/background to `assets/images/<source-basename>`, keyed only by the source file's basename. Two assets whose source files merely shared a basename (e.g. `player/idle.png` and `enemy/idle.png`, imported from different folders) **overwrote each other on disk** and clobbered each other's path/frame-metadata map entries — one sprite silently rendered as the other's image.

A single helper, `_exported_asset_filename(asset_name, file_path)`, now keys the exported filename on the **project-unique asset name + source extension**. The care here was consistency — the exported path appears in *six* places that must all agree, or the game loads a file that isn't there:
- the three path maps + the `sprite_meta_map` key (constructor),
- the three copy sites,
- `_generate_object`'s initial-sprite path,
- the room-render background filename.

I applied it at all of them, then let the full suite catch drift — which it did: the **L22 test** legitimately failed because a non-PNG background now exports as `bg_sky.jpg` (name + real extension) instead of `sky_photo.jpg`. That's correct — L22's actual intent (keep the real extension, don't hardcode `.png`) is preserved — so I updated the assertion to the new form.

**3 new tests** exporting same-basename sprites + a background to distinct files with verified bytes. **Suite: 1944 → 1947 passed, 0 failed.**
