## Unit 1 — geometry + first-person baseline

`samples/raycast_2/` now exists as a playable Doom-style maze on the finished raycast engine — no engine changes, pure sample authoring.

**Geometry decision, made on the ground:** maze_2's wall system (corner/horizontal/vertical segments) doesn't map cleanly to raycast_1's edge model, so rather than force a conversion I built the level from a **hand-controlled recursive-backtracker maze** (15×15 cells / 480×480, seeded for reproducibility, +10 knocked-through walls for loops and longer sightlines) and ran it through raycast_1's *exact* edge-wall placement formulas — which I reverse-derived from the geometry: `obj_wall_h` at `(c·32, r·32−4)`, `obj_wall_v` at `(c·32−4, r·32)`. Result: 198 instances (80 h-walls + 116 v-walls + player + goal), a guaranteed-solvable perfect maze with player and goal at opposite corners. I noted in the plan doc that this supersedes its "derive from maze_2" line.

**Assembly:** reused raycast_1's objects and sprites verbatim — including `obj_person`'s create-event `enable_raycast_view` and (critically) the empty `collision_with_obj_wall_h/_v` handlers that gate the engine's wall-blocking, a lesson baked into raycast_1's own tests. Renamed the project, single room, honest WIP README.

**Verified through the real `GameRunner.run()` loop** (so the raycast render path actually initializes, not a hand-built room that'd pass for the wrong reason): the textured first-person camera enables with the full wall/sky/floor config, `facing_angle` turns on input, and the player is stopped by walls instead of escaping the room. **3 new tests; suite 1947 → 1950, 0 failed.**

## Where we are

We're at ~73% usage and this is a clean, committed unit-boundary. The remaining sequence from the plan:
- **Unit 2** — collectible gems + score
- **Unit 3** — patrolling billboard monster
- **Unit 4** — gem-gated exit
- **Unit 5** (optional) — second themed room
- **Unit 6** — ship (README polish, thumbnails, Welcome-tab + translations, smoke runner, 3-target playtest)

Units 2–3 need the two new billboard sprites (`spr_gem`, `spr_monster`). Per the plan, I can bootstrap those by reusing `maze_3`'s `sprite_monster1` and a gem/coin sprite so the mechanics work now, and you reskin later — or you can author the art first. Want me to continue into **Unit 2** with bootstrapped art, or pause here so you can provide the gem/monster sprites?

The full-suite run plus the headless verification runs are the expensive part, and at ~14% left they could easily exhaust the budget mid-unit.

The saving grace: everything is committed per-unit, so a mid-Unit-2 stall loses only uncommitted Unit-2 work — next session resumes cleanly from Unit 1 on `main`. So it's *recoverable*, just wasteful.

Two sensible paths:

- **Recommended — stop here.** Unit 1 is a clean, pushed boundary. Start Unit 2 fresh next session with full budget and no risk of a stranded half-unit.
- **Lean Unit 2 attempt.** I keep it cheap: reuse an existing sprite for `spr_gem` (no art generation — copy `maze_2`'s diamond or `spr_goal`), a minimal `obj_gem`, a few gems, score wiring, and gate on **just the `raycast_2` test file** (fast/cheap) instead of the full 1950-test suite — noting in the commit that the full-suite gate runs first thing next session. That trades a bit of the usual discipline for fitting in the budget.

My recommendation is to **stop at the Unit 1 boundary** — it's the lower-regret choice. But if you'd like the lean attempt, say so and I'll move fast and bail cleanly (committing whatever's complete) if the budget gets thin. Which do you want?

## Unit 4 — gem-gated exit

`obj_goal` now gates the win on collecting everything. I rewrote its `collision_with_obj_person` (dropping raycast_1's inherited next-room/`set_health` cruft and the single-room-irrelevant `keyboard_press` debug keys) as maze_3's proven `test_instance_count` + `start_block`/`end_block` pattern:
- **All gems collected** → *"Bravo ! Tu as trouvé la sortie avec toutes les gemmes."* + `restart_game`
- **Gems remain** → *"Ramasse toutes les gemmes avant de sortir !"* (no win)

A couple of things I verified rather than assumed:
- **Param key `number`, not `count`** — maze_3's door uses `count`, but the desktop runtime actually *ignores* it (reads `number`, defaulting to 0) and only works by coincidence. I checked all three exporters: HTML5 reads `number`, Kivy reads `number`→`count`, desktop reads `number` — so `number` is the cross-target-correct key.
- **French accents are real UTF-8 in the file** (bytes `c3 a9` = é, no mojibake) — the `trouv�` you'd see in my console output was just Windows cp1252 stdout, not the file.

**+2 tests** (12 total): the gate blocks with gems present and opens with the win when all collected (through the real event dispatch), plus an accented-French assertion. **Suite 1957 → 1959, 0 failed.**

## Unit 5 — second themed room

`raycast_2` is now a two-level game. The second room is a cool **"crystal cavern"** — blue-tinted `*_ice` variants of the wall/sky/floor textures — reached by completing the first (warm brick) room.

The interesting engineering was **per-room theming**. Since `obj_person` (the camera) is the same object in both rooms, I couldn't bake the theme into it. The solution:
- Moved `enable_raycast_view` off `obj_person` onto tiny invisible **camera-controller objects** — `obj_cam0` (warm) in room0, `obj_cam1` (ice) in room1.
- Each controller names `obj_person` as the camera via the action's `camera_object` param — and I verified all three targets honor an explicit `camera_object` (runtime line 4433, HTML5 `params.camera_object || this.name`, Kivy `_find_raycast_camera` name lookup) before relying on it, so the *player* stays the camera even though the *controller* fires the action.

For the room transition I deliberately **avoided a fragile shared-goal if/else**: `obj_goal` (room0) → `next_room`; a separate `obj_goal_final` (room1) → win. Both gem-gated.

Verified through the real loop: room0 warm with `obj_person` as camera → collect all gems + goal → room1 renders with the ice theme. **+2 tests, and the two Unit-4 goal tests updated for the advance/win split. Suite 1959 → 1961, 0 failed.**

## 2. In-view HUD plan — `docs/RAYCAST_HUD_PLAN.md`

I grounded it by tracing the render/draw-event flow on all three targets first, so the hook points are concrete:
- **Root cause** — each renderer draws the first-person view and *returns before* the per-instance draw-event pass that composites the HUD (desktop `_render_room` ~1670, HTML5 `render(ctx)` ~3213, Kivy's opaque `canvas.after` overlay).
- **Design** — composite the existing draw pass (`draw_score`/`draw_lives`/`draw_text`, the maze_3 controller pattern — no new actions) as a **final screen-space pass on top of the finished 3D frame**. World-space top-down draws are explicitly scoped out.
- **Per-target hooks** — desktop: a `_render_draw_events` helper called after `_render_raycast_view`; HTML5: the draw-queue block after `renderRaycastView`; Kivy: a later `_raycast_hud_group` on `canvas.after` above the opaque overlay (with the `.format()` brace-doubling landmine flagged).
- **Unit sequence** — 0 plan → 1 desktop → 2 HTML5 → 3 Kivy → 4 parity test + wire a HUD controller into `raycast_2`/`raycast_1` and drop their "caption-only" caveats.

`RAYCAST_2_5D_PLAN.md`'s HUD item now links to it.

That closes out the raycast_2 arc cleanly: the sample is shipped, the session is recorded for the next machine, and the one remaining engine enhancement has a ready-to-execute plan. Whenever you want it, **Unit 1 (desktop HUD pass)** is the entry point.

> When the player moves at an angle towards a wall, the wall is drawn as if the is a corner. And when moving forwards or backwards, the "corner" moves as wel util it really is a corner

### Claude

That's a real rendering artifact worth diagnosing rather than guessing at. A false "corner" that *slides along the wall as you move* points at a distance discontinuity in the ray fan — most likely a diagonal leak at thin-wall junctions or a side/shading flip. Let me reproduce it against the real `_cast_ray` with a controlled flat wall:

A pure flat wall is smooth — so the artifact needs the real maze's mixed h/v walls. Let me scan the actual raycast_2 geometry for discontinuities:

Too noisy — real corners legitimately jump. The symptom "drawn *as if* there's a corner" on a flat surface points at a **shading crease**: adjacent columns hitting the same flat wall but getting different `side` values flips the half-brightness shading. Let me test for that specifically — smooth distance but flipped side:

**Found the mechanism**: 60,632 cases where adjacent columns hit at essentially the same distance but `side` flips — which flips the half-brightness shading, drawing a hard vertical crease on a continuous surface. That's precisely "drawn as if there is a corner," and it moves with the camera.

Let me confirm visually by rendering an actual frame at an angle to a wall:

Let me isolate definitively — render a **single long flat wall** viewed at an angle (if a flat surface shows a crease, it's a bug; if not, the user is seeing real h/v junctions):

The isolated flat wall renders **perfectly smooth** (uniform `side=1`, no crease) — so the DDA and shading are correct on a genuine flat surface. That rules out a core rendering bug and points somewhere more specific. Let me check one structural difference between the two samples' wall models:

I've traced it. Here's what the evidence shows:

## Diagnosis

**It's not a DDA/geometry bug.** I isolated a single long flat wall and rendered it at an angle — it comes out perfectly smooth (uniform `side=1`, clean receding wedge, no crease). The distance fan across a flat wall has zero kinks at any angle.

**The culprit is the binary side-shading.** The renderer shades every wall by which DDA axis was crossed — `side==1` (horizontal faces) is drawn at **half brightness**, `side==0` at full. Scanning the real maze I found **60,632 camera positions/angles where two adjacent screen columns hit at essentially the same distance (<3 px apart) but with `side` flipped**. So you get a hard 2:1 brightness discontinuity with *no depth change behind it* — the eye reads that as a corner, because a corner is the only thing that normally produces a brightness break.

That also explains your "it moves until it really is a corner": the h-wall/v-wall boundary slides across the screen as you move (that part is correct perspective), but because the shading is binary and harsh, it reads as a corner the *whole* way along — only occasionally landing where a real geometric corner is.

Worth noting: that 50% figure was itself a previous over-correction — the code comment says it was raised from 75% because 75% "read as barely distinguishable." So the contrast got pushed too far the other way.

## Fix options

| | Approach | Cost |
|---|---|---|
| **A** | Soften side-shading (50% → ~75–80%) | One constant per target (3 files) |
| **B** | Replace with **distance-based** shading (darken with range) | 3-target change; surfaces read continuous, depth still cued |
| **C** | **Both** — mild side shading (~85%) + distance falloff | 3-target change, best result |

I'd recommend **C**: distance falloff is what actually sells depth in a raycaster, and it lets the side-shading drop to a subtle hint that no longer fakes corners. **B** alone is also clean.

This is an engine change across desktop/HTML5/Kivy (with the parity test to keep them in sync), so it's a proper unit of work — and since you've weighed in on shading strength before, I don't want to unilaterally flip it back.

**Which would you like?** Say the word and I'll implement it as a scoped unit (one commit, per-target + parity test), then you can playtest the new look.

## What it was

Not a DDA bug — I confirmed an isolated flat wall renders perfectly smooth at any angle. The culprit was the **shading model**: a y-face was drawn at *half* brightness, so wherever an h-wall met a v-wall at roughly the same distance you got a hard **2:1 brightness break with no depth change behind it** — and the eye reads a brightness break with no depth cue as a corner. I measured **60,632** camera/angle combinations in raycast_2 where adjacent columns hit within 3px of the same distance but with `side` flipped. Since that junction slides across the screen as you move (correct perspective), the false corner appears to travel along the wall — exactly your "it moves until it really is a corner."

## The fix (option C)

One shared formula, implemented identically on all three renderers:

```
side_factor = 0.85 if side == 1 else 1.0      (was a binary 0.5)
dist_factor = 1 - 0.55 * clamp(corrected/max_dist, 0, 1)
shade       = max(0.35, side_factor * dist_factor)
```

The side hint is now a whisper rather than a 2:1 slam, and real distance falloff carries the depth. Applied to both the textured and flat-colour paths in `GameRoom._wall_shade` (desktop), `engine.js wallShade`, and the Kivy scene's `_wall_shade`.

Here's the same view before and after — the left wall no longer reads as a dark slab meeting a bright one; it recedes smoothly and the junction stops looking like a crease.

## Verification

- **Parity locked**: desktop vs Kivy `_wall_shade` numerically identical (<1e-12) across sides and distances, constants asserted equal, HTML5's constants/formula pinned structurally (no JS engine in CI).
- Six older tests asserted the exact half-brightness pixel values; I rewrote them to express the *new intent* (y-face subtly darker, not halved; hue preserved; distance darkens) rather than swapping in fresh magic numbers.
- **Full suite 1964 passed, 0 failed**; Kivy export still compiles with the model baked in.

The three constants are all in one place per target if you want to tune the feel further — `RAYCAST_FOG_STRENGTH` is the main dial for how much the distance darkens. Give it a playtest and tell me if you want the fog stronger or the side hint pushed further either way.

```python

...
x0 = int(col * col_width)                                  # uniform in SCREEN X
```

The renderer samples rays at **uniform angles** but draws them at **uniform screen positions**. That's not a perspective projection — it curves straight walls, which reads as a corner. Tellingly, the **floor-cast code in this same file already uses the correct camera-plane method** (its comment even calls it "the fisheye-correct equivalent of the wall pass" — but the wall pass never actually did it). All 8 pass. Full-suite gate:

1966 passed, 0 failed. Let me verify the Kivy export still compiles, then commit:

That screenshot pinned it — thank you, it was a genuine geometry bug, not the shading. Fixed and pushed (`fa8e499`).

## Root cause

The wall pass **wasn't a perspective projection**. It sampled rays at *uniform angles*:

```python
ray_offset = -fov/2 + fov * (col / num_columns)
```

…but drew each column at *uniform screen x*. Mapping equal angles onto equal screen spacing **curves straight lines** — the wall bows outward, which the eye reads as a corner that slides as you move.

I measured it precisely: for a plane, `1/corrected_distance` must be exactly **linear** across evenly-spaced columns. It had a **36% slope spread** — mathematically bent.

The tell was already in the codebase: the **floor cast** uses the correct camera-plane method, and its comment calls it *"the fisheye-correct equivalent of the wall pass"* — but the wall pass never actually did it. So walls and floor were subtly inconsistent too.

## Fix

Sample rays evenly on the **camera plane**, which is what evenly-spaced screen columns require:

```python
camera_x   = 2*(col+0.5)/num_columns - 1      # -1..1 across the screen
ray_offset = atan(tan(fov/2) * camera_x)      # not a linear ramp
```

Billboards got the matching inverse (`camera_x = tan(rel_angle)/tan(fov/2)`) so sprites track the walls instead of drifting toward the edges. Applied identically on desktop, HTML5 and Kivy — walls, floor and billboards now finally share one projection.

**Slope spread: 36% → 0.0%.** Here's the same view rendered after the fix — the brick courses now run dead straight to the vanishing point:

## Verification

Added a parity test that pins the load-bearing property: **a flat wall must project perfectly straight** (curvature < 1e-9 via second differences, normalized so it also holds for a perpendicular wall), plus a check that all three sources use the camera-plane mapping and the bent linear ramp is gone. **Suite 1966 passed, 0 failed**; Kivy export still compiles.

Worth noting the earlier shading change (`9ad00ef`) was still worthwhile — it removed the harsh 2:1 brightness break — but it was treating a symptom. This is the actual defect. Give it a play and let me know how the corridors read now.

A correct raycaster crops the texture to the visible slice (you see fewer, *bigger* bricks as you approach).

This explains everything you reported: columns that clamp show the whole texture compressed while neighbours don't → a hard break in the brick courses on a flat wall; and the clamp boundary **moves as you walk forward/back** — exactly "the corner moves as well." Let me fix it:

Found it — and your screenshot is what pinned it. Pushed as `499a60e`.

## The actual root cause

Your screenshot showed a **close-up** wall, which is the tell I'd been missing. The strip height was clamped to the screen:

```python
strip_h = min(h, int(h * cell_size / corrected))   # clamped
...
strip = scale(full_texture_column, (strip_w, strip_h))   # whole texture squeezed in
```

When a wall gets close enough to overflow the screen, that clamp kicks in — and the **entire brick texture gets compressed into the clamped strip**, while neighbouring columns that are slightly farther away don't clamp and render correctly. That's a hard break in the brick courses **across a flat wall**.

And critically: *which* columns clamp depends on distance — so the break **marches along the wall as you walk toward or away from it**. That is precisely the "corner that moves until it really is a corner" you described the first time. I measured it on a real close-up: **14 of 64 columns** were overflowing and squeezing.

## The fix

A correct raycaster **crops** the texture to the visible span (you see fewer, *bigger* bricks as you approach) rather than squeezing the whole thing:

```
full_h = h * cell_size / corrected      # TRUE, unclamped
v0, v1 = visible fraction of the strip
→ sample texture rows [v0·th, v1·th) into the visible height
```

Applied to all three renderers (desktop, HTML5 `drawImage srcY/srcH`, Kivy `get_region` with bottom-origin `src_y` for its y-up textures).

## Verification

Beyond the render above, I added a behavioural test: across the clamp boundary, the wall's centre line must map to the **same texture row** (v == 0.5 to 1e-9) — a squeezed strip drifts. Plus source checks that the squeeze signature is gone from all three. **Suite 1967 passed, 0 failed.**

## Honest accounting

You were right to push back twice. My first two fixes were real but secondary — the harsh 2:1 shading (`9ad00ef`) and the uniform-angle projection (`fa8e499`, which genuinely was bending walls, 36% → 0%). Neither was *this*. I should have gone to a close-up render earlier instead of testing flat walls at mid-distance, where the clamp never triggers. The "completely rethink" push is what got me to question the strip drawing itself rather than the ray maths.

Worth noting: **billboards have the same clamping bug** (`sprite_h = min(h, ...)`), so close-up gems/monsters will squeeze the same way. I left it out of this commit to keep the fix focused — say the word and I'll do it as a follow-up.

## Why it was jagged

With cropping in place, a close wall shows only a *few* texture rows magnified to fill the screen — **one texel spans 30–60 screen pixels**. My crop rounded the source row to a whole texel *per column*, so two adjacent columns landing either side of a texel boundary jumped by a full texel. That's the stair-stepping along every mortar line.

## The fix, per target

Each renderer already had a natural sub-texel mechanism — I just wasn't using it:

- **Desktop (pygame)** — `subsurface` needs integer rows, so crop to the *floor* texel plus a margin and carry the sub-texel remainder as a **blit source offset**. Residual error drops from a whole texel to ≤1px.
- **HTML5** — `drawImage` already samples float source rects, so I simply stopped rounding `srcY`/`srcH`. Canvas interpolates.
- **Kivy** — take the full-height 1-px column and select the visible slice with **float `tex_coords`** instead of an integer `get_region` (which snaps to texels).

Here's the close-up now — the mortar lines run as clean diagonals with no stepping:

**Suite 1967 passed, 0 failed**; Kivy export compiles; `engine.js` brace-balanced.

## Where the raycast renderer stands

Four real defects found and fixed from your playtesting, in dependency order:
1. `9ad00ef` — harsh binary 2:1 side shading (false-corner *reading*)
2. `fa8e499` — uniform-angle sampling → **bent walls** (36% → 0% curvature)
3. `499a60e` — screen-clamped strips squeezing the whole texture (**the** "corner that moves")
4. `b5b9beb` — texel-snapped crop → jagged close-ups

Your persistence on #3 was the one that mattered — I'd have stopped at #2 otherwise.

One known leftover I mentioned: **billboards still have the old clamping bug** (`sprite_h = min(h, ...)`), so a gem or monster you walk right up to will squeeze the same way walls used to. It's the same fix applied to the billboard pass. Want me to do that now, or leave it — it only shows when you're practically touching a sprite.

sprite_h = min(h, int(h * height / corrected))   # clamped...

## The raycast renderer now

Five defects found through your playtesting, all fixed and parity-locked across desktop/HTML5/Kivy:

| | Fix |
|---|---|
| `9ad00ef` | Harsh binary 2:1 side shading → subtle hint + distance falloff |
| `fa8e499` | Uniform-angle sampling → camera-plane projection (bent walls) |

All green. Committing:

Fixed and pushed (`5eca0b8`).

## Why they were cut in half

The raycast pass computed geometry from **raw `inst.x/y`**, but rendering and collision both place a sprite at **`x - origin_x`**. So for a sprite with a *centred* origin, the raycast pass was off by half a sprite.

`spr_gem`/`spr_monster` had origin **(16,16)** on a 32×32 — which put their billboard centres **exactly on the 32px grid lines, where the walls are**. The per-column occlusion test then correctly hid whichever half fell behind the wall. That's the slicing.

`obj_goal` has origin (0,0), which is why only the *new* billboards showed it — a good tell that this was introduced with the gems/monsters, not longstanding.

## Two-part fix

**Engine (correct for any project, not just this sample):** added `_sprite_top_left` / `GameRoom.spriteTopLeft` and routed **all three** raycast geometry sites through it on desktop and HTML5 — wall derivation, camera origin, and billboard centre. Any user who sets a centred origin would have hit this.

Kivy has **no** sprite-origin concept anywhere (it positions at raw `x,y`), so its raycast pass is already internally consistent — I left it alone rather than introduce a discrepancy.

**Sample:** gave the two billboard sprites a top-left origin (0,0) matching `obj_person`/`obj_goal`/`obj_wall`, and shifted their instances −16,−16 to preserve the cell centre. That also keeps all three export targets in agreement, since Kivy ignores origin.

Gem with a clear line of sight, rendering whole:

## Verification

- Every gem/monster centre is now **off** the grid lines (asserted for all instances)
- Camera centre unchanged (48,48) — the fix didn't disturb the player
- **Suite 1968 passed, 0 failed**; HTML5 + Kivy both export; smoke **15/15**
- Tests updated: top-left-origin check for both billboard sprites, plus a guard that no billboard ever sits on a wall line

That guard is the useful one going forward — if anyone repositions gems or monsters later, it'll catch a return of this bug immediately.

## [2026-07-20] User

> How are the messages generated because they must correspond to programmable actions in which case the person or student is the one writing the message in their own language

### Claude

Really good question — it goes to whether my change was even the right call. Let me verify the student-authoring path concretely before I answer:

Verified — and this is the crux of your question. Here's how it actually works:

## Two different authoring situations

**A student writing their own game** — nothing translates, and nothing should:

```
[en] student text shown unchanged: True
[fr] student text shown unchanged: True
[de] student text shown unchanged: True
[ru] student text shown unchanged: True
```

They add a `show_message` action, type *"Bravo ! Tu as gagné !"*, and that exact text shows in every IDE language. `execute_show_message_action` only substitutes when a `message_translations` dict exists — a student's action has none, so their words are always shown verbatim. That's correct and my change didn't touch it.

**An author shipping to many languages** — the IDE has an opt-in mechanism for this. Next to the message field in the action dialog there's a **"Translations…"** button (`MessageTranslationsDialog`) that writes `message_translations = {fr: …, de: …}` alongside the message. The engine convention is: `message` is the **English base**, and any non-English IDE resolves from the dict.

## Why I used it for `raycast_2`

A bundled sample isn't one person's game — it ships to students in 7 locales, so it has no single "author's language." That's exactly the case the mechanism was built for, and it's the same pattern the sample *names* already use ("Raycast — Level 2" → "Lancer de rayons — Niveau 2").

## But you've put your finger on a real trade-off

Because the engine forces the base to be English (it only looks up the dict when `lang != 'en'`), a **French student who opens the sample to learn from it sees English in the action editor** — the French is one click away behind "Translations…". For software whose primary audience is French-speaking students reading samples as teaching material, that's a genuine cost.

So it's your call, and both are defensible:

- **Keep it** — the *game* speaks each student's language; the editor shows English. Best for a sample shipped internationally.
- **Revert to plain French** (no translations dict) — the sample reads as a French student would actually write it, in both the editor and the game, at the cost of German/Spanish/etc. players seeing French.

There's a third consideration pushing toward "revert": **the exporters ignore `message_translations` entirely**, so an exported HTML5/Kivy build currently shows the English base to everyone. If the sample is mainly played via Test Game and export, plain French may serve your students better than a mechanism that only half-works.

Which would you like? If you keep it, I'd suggest I also close the exporter gap so the translations survive export.

> 
> 2. **Key Technical Concepts:**
>    - **Raycast 2.5D rendering (DOOM/Wolfenstein)**: DDA raycasting, thin edge-walls, fisheye correction, camera-plane projection, texture-U sampling, panning sky, low-res floor casting, billboard sprites with per-column occlusion
>    - **Camera-plane projection**: `ray_offset = atan(tan(fov/2) * camera_x)` where `camera_x = 2*(col+0.5)/N - 1` — required because uniform-angle sampling drawn at uniform screen x is NOT a perspective projection and bends straight walls
>    - **Texture cropping vs squeezing**: for walls overflowing the screen, crop the texture to the visible span (`full_h` unclamped, `v0/v1` fractions) rather than squeezing the whole texture into a clamped strip
>    - **Sub-texel sampling**: desktop carries the remainder as a blit source offset (`frac_px`, `texels_per_px`); HTML5 passes float `srcY`/`srcH` to `drawImage`; Kivy uses float `tex_coords`
>    - **Sprite origin awareness**: rendering/collision use `x - origin_x`; raycast geometry must too
>    - **Wall shading model**: `RAYCAST_SIDE_SHADE=0.85`, `RAYCAST_FOG_STRENGTH=0.55`, `RAYCAST_MIN_SHADE=0.35` (replaced a binary 0.5 half-brightness)
>    - **Three-renderer parity**: desktop `runtime/game_runner.py`, HTML5 `export/HTML5/templates/engine.js`, Kivy `export/Kivy/kivy_exporter.py` share no code; locked by `tests/test_raycast_export_parity.py`
>    - **Kivy landmines**: scene class is a `.format()` template (literal `{`/`}` must be doubled); textures are bottom-origin; no sprite-origin concept; top-level imports break other tests' stub-kivy envs
>    - **GameMaker event semantics**: `create` re-runs on `restart_room`; `game_start` fires once; `enable_raycast_view` must stay in `create` (camera re-arms per room entry)
>    - **`test_instance_count` param key is `number`, not `count`** (all three targets read `number`)
>    - **`message_translations`**: `message` is the English base; runtime resolves `message_translations[lang]` when `lang != 'en'`. Only the DESKTOP runtime honors it — engine.js and Kivy ignore it
>    - **Guide i18n**: `README.<lang>.md` next to `README.md`, resolved by `SampleDocsDialog.guide_path()` with English fallback
>    - **Session-limit discipline**: one task ≈ one commit, commit+push after each, no multi-agent workflows, no feature branches
> 
> 3. **Files and Code Sections:**
> 
>    - **`runtime/game_runner.py`** (desktop reference renderer)
>      - `GameRoom._sprite_top_left(inst)` — origin-aware top-left, added to fix billboards sliced by walls:
>        ```python
>        @staticmethod
>        def _sprite_top_left(inst):
>            spr = getattr(inst, 'sprite', None)
>            ox = getattr(spr, 'origin_x', 0) or 0 if spr else 0
>            oy = getattr(spr, 'origin_y', 0) or 0 if spr else 0
>            return inst.x - ox, inst.y - oy
>        ```
>        Used at 3 sites: `_build_raycast_walls` (line ~1833), camera (~2010), billboard centre (~2178)
>      - `_wall_shade` classmethod + `RAYCAST_SIDE_SHADE/FOG_STRENGTH/MIN_SHADE` constants
>      - Camera-plane wall loop: `plane_tan = math.tan(fov_rad / 2)`; `camera_x = 2.0 * (col + 0.5) / num_columns - 1.0`; `ray_offset = math.atan(plane_tan * camera_x)`
>      - Wall strip with crop + sub-texel:
>        ```python
>        full_h = h * cell_size / max(corrected, 1e-4)
>        y_top = half_h - full_h / 2.0
>        y0 = max(0, int(math.floor(y_top))); y1 = min(h, int(math.ceil(y_top + full_h)))
>        vis_h = y1 - y0
>        texels_per_px = th / full_h
>        src_y_f = (y0 - y_top) * texels_per_px
>        src_y = max(0, min(th - 1, int(math.floor(src_y_f))))
>        frac_px = (src_y_f - src_y) / texels_per_px
>        need = int(math.ceil(vis_h * texels_per_px)) + 2
>        src_h = max(1, min(th - src_y, need))
>        dest_h = max(1, int(round(src_h / texels_per_px)))
>        ...
>        off = max(0, min(dest_h - 1, int(round(frac_px))))
>        screen.blit(strip, (x0, y0), (0, off, strip_w, min(vis_h, dest_h - off)))
>        ```
>      - Billboard pass got the identical treatment (`full_h`, `rows_per_px`, `frac_px`, `blit_h`)
>      - `execute_set_draw_font_action` gained `_GM_FONT_ALIGN_FALLBACK` module constant
> 
>    - **`export/HTML5/templates/engine.js`**
>      - Module-level `RAYCAST_SIDE_SHADE = 0.85`, `RAYCAST_FOG_STRENGTH = 0.55`, `RAYCAST_MIN_SHADE = 0.35` before `class GameRoom`
>      - `wallShade(side, corrected, maxDist)`, `_shadeColor(hex, factor)`, `GameRoom.spriteTopLeft(inst)`, `_textureData(sprite)`, `castFloorPlane(...)`
>      - Camera-plane: `const planeTan = Math.tan(fovRad / 2)`, `const cameraX = 2.0*(col+0.5)/numCols - 1.0`, `const rayOffset = Math.atan(planeTan * cameraX)`
>      - Wall draw: `ctx.drawImage(texSprite, texX, srcY, 1, srcH, x0, y0, stripW, visH)` with float srcY/srcH
>      - Billboard: `ctx.drawImage(img, srcX, bSrcY, 1, bSrcH, screenX, by0, 1, bVisH)`
>      - Verified brace/paren balance vs HEAD after every edit (deltas always 0)
> 
>    - **`export/Kivy/kivy_exporter.py`** (`.format()` template — braces doubled)
>      - Scene methods: `_build_raycast_walls`, `_cast_ray`, `_wall_shade`, `_raycast_color`, `_raycast_texture`, `_raycast_texture_pixels`, `_billboard_texture`, `_floor_buffer`, `_render_floor_plane`, `_find_raycast_camera`, `_render_raycast`
>      - `_exported_asset_filename(asset_name, file_path)` module helper (name-keyed export filenames)
>      - Wall texture slice via float `tex_coords=(0.0, v0, 1.0, v0, 1.0, v1, 0.0, v1)`
>      - `from kivy.graphics.texture import Texture` is a **local** import inside `_render_floor_plane`
> 
>    - **`tests/test_raycast_export_parity.py`** — 9+ tests: exact desktop↔Kivy `_cast_ray` over 260 rays, `_wall_shade` parity, flat-wall straightness (curvature < 1e-9 via second differences), camera-plane in all three sources, crop + sub-texel signatures, billboard signatures, facing-angle convention
> 
>    - **`samples/raycast_2/`** — complete two-level game: `obj_person` (create=`enable_raycast_view` removed→controllers; `game_start`=set_score/set_lives; collision handlers), `obj_cam0`/`obj_cam1` (per-room themes naming `camera_object='obj_person'`), `obj_gem`, `obj_monster`, `obj_goal` (→next_room), `obj_goal_final` (→win), `obj_wall_h/v`, rooms room0/room1, `README.md` + `README.fr.md`
> 
>    - **`widgets/welcome_tab.py`** — `SAMPLE_PROJECTS` (added raycast_1, raycast_2), and new guide i18n:
>      ```python
>      @staticmethod
>      def _guide_language() -> str:
>          try:
>              from core.language_manager import get_language_manager
>              lang = get_language_manager().get_current_language()
>          except Exception:
>              lang = None
>          return (lang or "en").split("_")[0].lower()
> 
>      def guide_path(self, relative_path: str) -> Path:
>          base = self._repo_root / relative_path
>          lang = self._guide_language()
>          if lang and lang != "en":
>              localised = base / f"README.{lang}.md"
>              if localised.is_file():
>                  return localised
>          return base / "README.md"
>      ```
> 
>    - **French guides created**: `samples/raycast_2/README.fr.md`, `maze_1`, `maze_2`, `maze_3`, `maze_4`, `plateforme_1` — all with proper accents, technical identifiers left untranslated

> 4. **Errors and fixes:**
>    - **`GameRunner` has no `_cast_ray`** — it's on `GameRoom`. Fixed import/constructor in the parity test.

## Four session-size bites

| Session | Content | Est. |
|---|---|---|
| **A** | Unit 0 plan, Unit 1 desktop HUD, Unit 2 HTML5 HUD | ~50% |
| **B** | Unit 3 Kivy HUD, Unit 4 parity + retire the caveat | ~45% |
| **C** | Unit 5 maze+objects, Unit 6 health loop + status bar | ~50% |
| **D** | Unit 7 room 2 + gated exit, Unit 8 ship it | ~50% |

Each unit is one commit, pushed before the next. **Sessions A–B leave the engine feature complete and shipped** — I made that a deliberate cut line so `raycast_3` can slip without leaving anything half-done.

## Two things I found by re-verifying rather than trusting the old plan

**The engine work is smaller than it looked.** `draw_score`, `draw_lives` and `draw_health_bar` already have full codegen *and* renderers on all three targets — desktop `_process_draw_queue`, [engine.js:2218](export/HTML5/templates/engine.js#L2218)/2240/2328, and [code_generator.py:1012](export/Kivy/code_generator.py#L1012)/1030/1118. Nothing new has to be authored per-action; this is purely a compositing-order change. That's what lets `raycast_3` lean hard on the HUD without engine risk.

**The Kivy unit is harder than the old plan implied**, with two traps it missed:

1. `_dq_group` lives on each *GameObject widget's* `canvas.after`, but `_raycast_group` lives on the *scene's* — which paints after all children. A group added to a child widget can never get above the overlay. The HUD needs its own group on the scene's canvas.
2. `_render_draw_queue` flips y against `room_height`. A screen-space HUD must flip against **window** height — reusing it unchanged puts the HUD at the wrong height on every scrolling raycast room.

I budgeted Unit 3 at ~30% accordingly and specified a test using a room whose height differs from the window, so the two can't be silently confused.

## Concept for raycast_3

**"The status bar is the game."** The mechanic that justifies it: monsters do **health damage** instead of instant death, so `draw_health_bar` is how you know you're in trouble — plus medkit pickups, score/lives, and a key counter making the gated exit readable instead of guessed.

## Two things I'd like your call on before Session C

I left these open in the plan rather than deciding for you:

- **Status-bar layout** — DOOM-style bottom bar vs. corner overlays. A bottom bar eats vertical view space, so it affects the room budget and the look. Worth a mock first.
- **Damage model** — per-touch fixed damage vs. damage-over-time while overlapping. I'd recommend per-touch with a short invulnerability alarm: simpler, and it matches `raycast_2`'s collision idiom.

Also noted as explicitly out of scope: a **minimap**. It's the obvious next ask, but it needs world-space projection — which this plan deliberately excludes — so it belongs in its own plan rather than quietly expanding this one.

The HUD now composites on desktop and HTML5. Both changes are the same shape: split the draw-event block out of the render method (`run_draw_event` / `runDrawEvent`), then call it after the raycast render instead of the bare `return`.

I verified every regression test **fails with the fix stashed** — two on desktop, three on HTML5 — so none of them pass for the wrong reason. The desktop pixel test compares against a no-HUD baseline run, so it can't pass on a pixel the raycast view happened to paint anyway.

## Two pre-existing divergences I found — worth your attention

Neither is caused by this work; both were sitting in the codebase and I'd have silently inherited them:

**1. Visibility.** Desktop's `render()` returns early on `not visible` *before* the draw event, so an invisible instance's draw event never fires. `engine.js` runs the draw event **regardless** of `visible` — only the sprite blit is skipped. So an invisible HUD controller works on web and silently does nothing on desktop.

**2. Depth order.** `engine.js` sorts instances **ascending**; the desktop runtime sorts **descending**. GameMaker semantics are "higher depth drawn first, so lower depth ends up in front" — which makes the desktop order right and engine.js's likely a real bug, affecting any game with overlapping draw events, not just raycast ones.

I deliberately did **not** fix either. Each is a behaviour change beyond a compositing unit and could alter existing exported games. I matched each target's own normal path so raycast and non-raycast agree *within* a target, and pinned both with comments and tests so they can't drift further. They belong in Unit 4, where the cross-target question sits — I'd like your call there rather than making it for you.

## One thing I fixed in my own work

Unit 1's guard test rasterised text, which depends on ambient pygame font state other tests can leave broken. It passed alone and failed in the full suite ("Text has zero width"). Now it records dispatch without rasterising; the pixel test still covers real rendering. Worth flagging because it's exactly the failure mode that looks like a regression and isn't.

**Session B** — Unit 3 (Kivy, the hard one: scene-level `canvas.after` group above the opaque overlay, plus the window-height vs room-height y-flip) and Unit 4 (parity + retiring the caption-only caveat, where I'd like your decision on the two divergences above).

## What the samples actually needed

You asked me to make sure the sample games work as intended, so I surveyed all 15 before touching anything. The result is worth knowing:

**Eleven samples use a single depth throughout** — they render identically under either sort, so they were never broken. **Four were genuinely wrong** on both export targets: `maze_3`, `maze_4`, `plateforme_3`, `treasure`. The clearest case is `plateforme_3`, where the player (depth −100) drew *behind* the level exit (depth 100). I pinned that survey with a test so adding a depth to another sample can't silently widen the blast radius unnoticed.

## The Kivy situation was worse than I reported

When I flagged this after Session A, I described it as engine.js sorting the wrong way. Digging in, **Kivy ignored `depth` entirely** — the word appeared nowhere in `export/Kivy/`. Instances drew in creation order, with no z-ordering at all. That needed depth plumbed end to end: emitted per generated object, defaulted on the base class, used for the draw-event loop, and used to order instances on insert.

The insert side has a wrinkle worth recording: widget order *is* z-order in Kivy, and it draws children in **reverse** (`children[0]` on top). So the child-widget path keeps `children` sorted **ascending** by depth, while the Fbo path (views-enabled rooms) inserts **descending**, since an Fbo renders in add order. Opposite directions, same intent.

## One judgement call

Three existing tests failed on my new `add_widget(instance, index=idx)` call. The stubs hardcoded index 0 and rejected the keyword — but **real Kivy's signature is `add_widget(widget, index=0, canvas=None)`**, so the stubs were modelling Kivy inaccurately rather than the exporter misusing it. I moved the six stubs to match real Kivy instead of working around them in the generated code. Worth flagging because the opposite call — bending the export to fit a wrong test double — would have quietly locked in the inaccuracy.

All five behaviour tests were confirmed to fail with the two export files stashed, so none pass for the wrong reason.

## Worth noting

These fixes change how four samples render on HTML5 and Android. The tests prove the ordering logic, but they can't prove it *looks* right — **a browser and Android playtest of `plateforme_3` would be worth doing** before the next release, since that's the one with five distinct depths.

Session B remains as planned: Unit 3 (Kivy HUD compositing) and Unit 4 (parity). Unit 4 just got smaller — its main open question was the two divergences now resolved.

## Both Kivy traps from the plan were real

Worth recording since the plan predicted them and they'd have been easy to get wrong:

**Z-order.** The per-instance `_dq_group` lives on each *GameObject widget's* canvas, while the opaque raycast overlay lives on the *scene's* — which paints over every child. A HUD group added to a child could never rise above it. It had to be scene-level.

**The y-flip.** `_render_draw_queue` flips against `room_height`, but a HUD is screen-space and must flip against the **window** height. Since `raycast_2`'s rooms are 480×480 in a 640×480 window, this one would have shown up immediately — but a room matching the window height would have hidden it until some later sample didn't. There's a test asserting `room_height` doesn't appear in that call.

## One authoring consequence worth knowing

`obj_hud` is a **separate object**, not a draw event bolted onto the camera controller — and that's load-bearing, not stylistic. `obj_cam0`/`obj_cam1` are `visible: false`, and since the visibility fix an invisible instance doesn't run its draw event on any target. Putting the HUD there would have silently drawn nothing. Both guides, the plan, and a test now say so, because this is exactly the kind of thing that costs an hour when building `raycast_3`.

## Still worth doing before a release

The depth fix changes how `maze_3`, `maze_4`, `plateforme_3` and `treasure` render on HTML5 and Android. The tests prove the ordering logic; they can't prove it *looks* right. **A browser and Android playtest of `plateforme_3`** (five distinct depths, and the one that was most visibly wrong) would close that out — it needs your eyes, not more tests.

## Next

Sessions C–D build `raycast_3` — the health/status-bar sample. Two open questions from the plan I'd still like your call on before Session C: **status-bar layout** (DOOM-style bottom bar vs. corner overlays) and **damage model** (I'd recommend per-touch with a short invulnerability alarm).

## [2026-07-20] User

> which would be cleaner and easier to implement - DOOM-style bottom bar vs. corner overlays?

### Claude

Let me verify one thing that decides this — whether the raycast view can render into a sub-region, or always fills the window.

**Corner overlays — decisively.** It's not close, and there's a specific reason that isn't obvious.

## The blocker for a bottom bar

The raycast renderer always fills the whole window (`w, h = screen.get_size()`) and hardcodes the horizon at `h / 2`. `enable_raycast_view` has no viewport parameter — I checked all 15 of them.

That leaves two ways to do a DOOM bar, and both are worse than they look:

**Paint an opaque bar over the bottom of the finished frame.** Works today with zero engine change — but the horizon stays at `h/2`, the true screen centre, while the *visible* view centre moves up. The result is a permanent slight-upward-tilt feel, because eye level now sits below the middle of what you can see. DOOM avoided this precisely by shrinking the 3D viewport rather than covering it. You'd also be floor-casting pixels you then hide, and the floor cast is the expensive pass.

**Do it properly — letterbox the view above the bar.** That means threading a viewport height through all three renderers. Horizon, wall-strip heights, floor casting, billboard scaling and sky panning all key off full screen height, so it touches every pass in three hand-written copies, plus parity tests. That's a real engine unit — roughly the size of Sessions A–B again — for a cosmetic change.

## What corner overlays cost

Nothing. It's what `raycast_2`'s `obj_hud` already does, shipped and tested an hour ago: `draw_score` top-left, `draw_lives` top-right. `raycast_3` adds `draw_health_bar` in a corner and a `draw_text` key counter. All four actions are already 3-target complete.

You also keep the full 640×480 of first-person view, which matters more in a raycast game than in a 2D one — the view *is* the game.

## Recommendation

Corner overlays for `raycast_3`. If you later want the DOOM look specifically for the period feel, that's a legitimate standalone engine feature — a `viewport_height` parameter on `enable_raycast_view` — and it'd slot in naturally as another session alongside the minimap, which needs screen-space real estate too. Deciding that later costs nothing; deciding it now costs a session before the sample exists.

One thing the corner layout should still get right: keep the score and health in **opposite** corners rather than stacked, so a wide health bar can't collide with a long score string as numbers grow.

## The blocker: `raycast_3`'s core mechanic was going to be desktop-only

Before wiring the health loop I checked what health actually needs on each target, and found the plan's risk assessment was **wrong**:

| | desktop | HTML5 | Kivy |
|---|---|---|---|
| `set_health`, `draw_health_bar` | ✅ | ✅ | ✅ |
| `test_health` | ✅ | ✅ | ❌ |
| `test_lives` | ✅ | ✅ | ❌ |
| `no_more_health` | ✅ | ❌ | ❌ |

The *display* half of health worked everywhere. The *logic* half didn't. On an export, the health bar would have moved correctly while every conditional branching on it silently vanished — a "you died" handler that never fires.

That's precisely the failure `raycast_3` exists to demonstrate against, and it would have looked fine in every test I'd have written against the desktop runtime. The plan's claim of "engine risk: none" came from a check that only looked at *draw* actions — I've corrected it in the doc along with the lesson: when a sample introduces a mechanic, verify the **conditionals and events** it needs on every target, not just the actions that draw it.

The fixes were small mirrors of code that already existed (`test_score`, `no_more_lives`), so I closed the gap as its own commit rather than folding it invisibly into Unit 6. One detail worth noting: both dispatches fire only on the **downward** `>0 → <=0` crossing, because without that guard a further `set_health` at zero re-fires the handler — a death loop rather than a death.

`test_lives` came along for free, which fixes lives conditionals for every other game on Android too.

## A judgement call I made

This was engine work during a sample session, which is beyond Session C's stated scope. I did it because `raycast_3` is blocked without it and the fix was mechanical — but if you'd rather I'd stopped and asked, tell me and I'll check in earlier on that kind of boundary next time.

Ready to continue with **Unit 6** — monsters damaging health per-touch with an invulnerability alarm, medkit pickups, and the corner-overlay HUD (score top-left, lives top-right, health bar bottom-left). Say the word and I'll carry on.

You said make sure everything works, so I drove the actual mechanics rather than just asserting the JSON looks right:

- **Desktop** — 4 touches empty the bar and cost **exactly one** life with health refilled; a re-touch inside the invulnerability window is blocked while one after it lands; the medkit heals 50→90 and is consumed; picking one up at full health stays capped at 100.

## Two things I got wrong along the way

Both worth recording because they cost real time and would cost it again:

**The seed was a bad level.** My first maze put the player's spawn cell walled to the east while they spawn *facing* east — you began the game nose-to-wall, moving 6px before stopping. It passed every structural test. I picked a seed where the start opens east and added `check_start()`, which asserts it on every generate, plus a behavioural test that holding Up must travel more than one cell.

**A debugging dead end I want to flag.** When a second monster hit didn't land, I spent a while suspecting my design. It turned out the runtime fires collision events on **enter** only, so a continuous overlap deals exactly one hit no matter how long it lasts — my test was pinning the player on the monster, which can't ever produce a second hit. The design was fine; the test was wrong. (Related: alarms and collisions dispatch via `execute_action_list`/`execute_collision_event`, *not* `execute_event`, so my first spy saw nothing and made it look like no events were firing at all.)

The invulnerability alarm still earns its place — it matters for the repeated touch/untouch of a monster patrolling *through* you, which is the real gameplay case.

## Two test problems I caught in my own work

**A test that would have passed while the thing it guards broke.** `test_the_hud_still_renders_in_room1` originally asserted only the room name — so if the HUD vanished after a room change, it would still have gone green. That's the exact regression it exists to catch, since a room change builds a fresh room with `raycast_camera=None`. It now records `(room, object, command-type)` triples during the run and asserts the HUD actually draws *while in room1*.

**A hardcoded count that was never the point.** `test_every_sample_ships_a_french_guide` asserted exactly 15 samples; `raycast_3` made it 16 and it failed. I relaxed it to a floor plus a per-sample check rather than bumping the number — otherwise it needs editing every time a sample lands, which trains people to edit the assertion instead of reading it.

**Nobody has watched `raycast_3` render.** I verified the exports *carry* the whole loop — `no_more_health`, `alarm_0`, `draw_health_bar`, `obj_hud` and `spr_medkit` all survive codegen — and the HUD-compositing tests pass per target. ## The design finding that changed the work

The HUD plan assumed each target would need a new minimap *renderer*. Checking first showed it doesn't: all three already cache the same derived wall-edge sets, all three already render `rectangle` and `line` commands, and the action can reach the room to read walls at queue time.

So `draw_minimap` is a **macro action** — it emits ordinary primitives. **No renderer changes on any target.** Only the geometry gets ported three times, and geometry is numerically comparable in a parity test exactly like `_cast_ray` already is. That makes Units 2–3 substantially smaller than planned.

## Two bugs I shipped and then caught

Worth being direct about, because the first one is the more instructive:

**The camera never resolved.** I called `room._find_raycast_camera` — which is a **Kivy-only** helper. On desktop `getattr` returned `None`, so no player marker was ever drawn. My first end-to-end test passed anyway, because it only asserted that wall lines appeared. The map looked right and the "you are here" marker simply wasn't there.

**The marker used the wrong point.** Raw `x`/`y` is the sprite's corner, but rays are cast from the origin-aware *centre* of the cell — so the marker sat half a sprite off the actual viewpoint. Same class of exact-grid-line error as the 2026-07-17 bug hunt.

Both were only found once I wrote a test asserting *where* the marker lands (1:1 map, within a pixel of the player's projected centre) instead of *that something was drawn*. That's the same trap as the room1 HUD test earlier this session — a test that passes while the feature is broken.

## Before I continue

I've been working a long stretch and haven't had a usage figure since 26% at Session C. Units 2–4 are each meaningful chunks — HTML5 port, Kivy port, then parity plus wiring the minimap into `raycast_3`'s free corner (bottom-right) with guide updates in both languages.

Tell me your usage and I'll either carry straight on through Units 2–4, or stop here — `main` is clean and every unit is committed and pushed, so nothing is lost either way.

## The finding that shrank the work

The HUD plan assumed each target needed a new minimap *renderer*. Checking first showed it didn't: all three already cache the same wall-edge sets and already render `rectangle`/`line` commands. So `draw_minimap` is a **macro action** that emits ordinary primitives — **no renderer changes on any target**, and the parity surface is pure geometry, comparable numerically to under 1e-9 exactly like `_cast_ray`.

Kivy still needed the two-halves pattern: its codegen emits single-line expressions, but a minimap needs loops, so the generator emits a *call* to a base-object method. I verified the call's arity matches the definition and both generated files compile — that mismatch is precisely what M34 was.

Worth restating because it's the same lesson twice in one day: in Unit 1 the camera never resolved (I used `_find_raycast_camera`, which is **Kivy-only** — desktop uses `_find_first_instance`), so **no player marker was ever drawn**. ## Your instinct found a real bug, not just a cost

At its old bottom-right position the minimap **overlapped Android's virtual D-pad by 117×47 px**. The D-pad lives bottom-right, which is exactly where I'd put it — so on Android it was partly hidden *under the controls*. Moved to the centre, and the test now computes the D-pad's extent *from the exporter template*, so the two can't silently collide again.

## What the toggle needed

`M` shows/hides it; off by default. While off it emits **nothing** — 248 wall lines when on, zero when off — so the per-frame cost is gone during play.

Getting there uncovered three pre-existing export gaps:

1. **Kivy's `key_map` held only the four arrow keys**, in three duplicated copies. Every other key generated `if key == 0:` and silently never fired on Android — while desktop and `engine.js` both handle letters. Now single-sourced with a–z, 0–9, space/enter/escape.
2. **The lookup was case-sensitive.** `maze_3` writes its debug keys as uppercase `N`/`P`, so those stayed dead even after the first fix. Its R/N/P room-skip keys now all work on Android.
3. **There was no way to press a non-arrow key on Android at all.** The D-pad gains a map button, emitted only when a game actually binds `m` — verified `raycast_3` gets one and `raycast_1` doesn't, so ordinary games don't grow a mystery control.

So the fix incidentally revived letter keys for every exported game, not just this one.

## One design detail worth knowing

The toggle uses `test_variable` + `exit_event` rather than two bare conditionals. The naive version sets the flag to 1, then immediately reads 1 and sets it straight back to 0 — the map never appears. Both guides document that, since anyone copying the pattern will hit it.

## On the pause you originally suggested

I went with toggle-and-no-pause as recommended, which matches Doom's automap. If you'd still like true pause later, it's a separate engine capability — no pause action exists today — and worth its own plan rather than being bolted on.

## Still needing your eyes

Unchanged and now the main outstanding item: nobody has *watched* any of this render. `raycast_3` in a browser and on Android, and `plateforme_3` after the depth fix. The Android map button in particular is drawn but never visually verified.

**Everything from this session — all the raycast HUD, export depth/visibility, `raycast_3`, minimap, and on-demand-map work — is committed and pushed.** Nothing of mine is outstanding. The working tree and `origin/main` are level.

The one modified file, `docs/session-notes/2026-07-03-884fd424.md`, is **not mine and not part of this work.** It's an auto-maintained transcript log — a readable record of past conversations, appended to by an extractor hook (you can see it: the diff is 5,649 lines of *prior* session dialogue about match3 work, the July audits, releases).
